O, my children, now I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine. By its completion at the end and by its explanations and clarifications within it, in my view, I believe I have rendered comprehensible to you the treatise on that part of practice that I love, namely surgery.
For surgery has been greatly neglected in our region and in our time, although the science of it still exists and its foundations remain. There are only small descriptions of it in the books of the Ancients, which have led many astray into error and hesitation because they misinterpret its intentions and weaken its power and art.
And it seems to me that I should enliven this treatise by organizing it according to the method of explanation and clarification and abbreviation; and so that the forms of cauterizing tools and other instruments used in the practice may become clear through additional explanation and preparation, which is necessary.
But the reason why a good practitioner is not readily found in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive and requires one to be well-versed in anatomy, which involves knowing the functions of the members and their forms, as well as their connections and separations; and to have knowledge of the bones, nerves, and ligaments, along with their numbers and structures; and of the pulsating and non-pulsating veins and their locations.
And for this reason Hippocrates said that in name there is much, but in deed little, and especially in the practice of the hand.
This we have already said in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not well versed in what we have said about anatomy will surely fall into error by which men are killed, as I have seen too many of them, for some are formed in this science and get themselves out of that with lack of knowledge and experience.
For I saw a mad physician who had opened an abscess on the neck of a woman, and cut some arteries in the neck so that much blood flowed out, by which the woman died.
And I saw another physician who claimed that he would remove a stone from a man who was suffering from kidney stones, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone along with part of the bladder, by which that man died on the third day.
And I had already examined and called upon to remove that stone, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I recognized that he would die.
And I saw another physician who was paid by a duke of our land to perform surgery, and he attended to a black castrated man who had a fracture in his thigh; and due to the doctor's ignorance, he exposed the fracture above the wound with splints and bandages in a strong structure, but did not leave room for swelling.
And he loosened it according to his desires, and soon he rebandaged it, and after some days commanded that we loosen the bandage; and thus it remained until the thigh and foot became abscessed, and he came to ruin.
And I was called to him, and prepared to loosen the bandage, and upon doing so, he found relief from his pains.
However, once corruption was confirmed in the limb, because it could not be restrained, and it did not cease to spread the corruption throughout the limb until the patient was in danger.
And I saw another surgeon who incised a cancerous abscess, and it ulcerated near the armpit, until the greatness of the evil became apparent to his lord.
The cause of which is that the malignant cancer is free of melancholic humor; therefore, it does not need to affect all the iron [bones], but rather be in the limb where it can be hosted by everything.
And for this reason, children, it is necessary for you that the operation be planned in two aspects: one is an operation associated with health, and the other is a procedure in which there is risk even under the best conditions.
And I have already stated this in every part of this book where an operation involves both error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to heed this and leave that, so that the readers do not find a way to criticize and defame you.
And so take up your weapons with vigilance and the protection of God, and treat your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best method by the grace of God to lead to health and praiseworthy success, and leave behind terrible diseases that are difficult to heal, and avoid those things that make you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not escape you: for it remains more for your glory, and much in this world and later in the praise of your sanctity.
Wound treatment in some monastic surgeries: Do not treat illness badly, for then bad doctors are not named.
And thus I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begin the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered about the head, in the forms of the instruments and in the tools of the cauteries, and of what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, about cutting and piercing, and about cupping, and bandaging, and extracting arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered regarding the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, about restoration and dislocation, and the treatment of twists and similar conditions; a chapter ordered concerning the head, and the forms of the instruments.
I shall describe the quality of the actions of that cautery and its ingredients, and in what condition cautery is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the speech about the quality of the actions of cautery and its ingredients is long and extensive, subtle by science, and secretly hidden; and many wise people have spoken about it extensively, and they are often discordant about it, but for me a little of their speeches suffices, fearing lengthiness.
Therefore, I say that cauteries are applied universally to all parts, whether they have substance or not, with the exception of two parts, which are tail-like parts without substance.
In the dry and solid tail-like part, there is disagreement; for some say that cauterization is helpful for it, while others say the opposite, that cauterization is not appropriate in a disease characterized by quality and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is inappropriate to treat a dry and solid ailment with something hot and dry.
And those who say the opposite, that cauterization is helpful for all dry and solid diseases that occur in human bodies, are correct, because when you compare the human body and its moisture to the nature of fire, you find that the human body is more fragile.
And I say according to the words of that person, that experience has already shown this to me on several occasions.
But I do not wish to go into that for one who has already been tested and exercised in the chapter of cauterization through previous exercise, and has known the diversity of human constitution and the disposition of diseases in their seasons, and the causes of those diseases and their accidents, and the duration of time for them.
But in the other parts, there is no need for fear on your part, especially in fragile and humid diseases, because all doctors are now in agreement about those, and they do not disagree about the benefit of cauterization in them.
And you know, children, that the secret of healing by fire and its goodness is that when it comes to cauterization with burning medicine, fire is simple, and its action affects only the member being cauterized, without affecting other members nearby except for minor harm.
Here is the English translation:
Keep the fire, by its height and by the goodness of its substance, does not do anything but superfluify. And it is known to us through experiment and through length of service in studying this art about the truth of causes; and for that I excuse myself from the length of speech. And if it were not read in my book at this point I would hide from you secrets with the fire, and the quality of its operation on bodies and the expulsion thereof by which illnesses are expelled, with philosophical demonstrative speech, which would sharpen your wits.
And understand, children, that they disagree about the time when it is convenient to perform cautery and they pause at the best time of spring.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by accidental circumstances of time is submerged in the benefit brought about by what is drawn from the cautery, and even more so if the cautery is for pains or necessary pain, such as strength and boils, which do not tolerate delay due to fear of malice that may arise, for it is better than a small harm entering through the wrong time.
And this does not cross your minds, or, children, what is valued only by the common people and foolish doctors is that for the illness which is cured by cautery it is not opportune at all times, and they pause in its consequence.
And the reason is not thus because they think it is so, for cautery is nothing but a substitute for medicine that alters the complexion and dries up the humors which are the cause of pains.
But still, cautery surpasses medicine in terms of the speed of its effectiveness and the virtue of its operation and the greatness of its power.
It is possible that his illness may return at some time according to the condition of the patient and the location of his illness, and its nature, and what is prepared in his body by the accumulation of superfluities within him, and what has been acquired through food and similar causes; but if the illness in which cauterization was administered is a subtle illness of few superfluities and humidities, such as cauterization of a tooth for pain and similar things, it is possible that that pain will not return in him, but this is done rarely.
And the common saying is that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, and it is said by practitioners, not because those who understand it think there is no cure in medicine or any other cause before cauterization, but because they believe that when all other remedies have been tried without success, cauterization may be necessary.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the understanding that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer methods of cure for certain illnesses, and those medicines do not help, and then at the end of the treatment cauterization is administered, and it proves effective for that. And here I say that cauterization is not the last remedy of medicine, not according to the understanding which the common people and the foolish physicians hold.
And the ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and its excellence in substance.
And they said that the place for cauterization does not matter, which is absolutely not true, because I have seen it proven and have observed that this does not work in some bodies but works well with others; cauterization with gold is good and better than with iron as they say. However, when you perform cauterization with fire using gold, it is not clearly defined for you, since the hardness of the gold determines its quantity needed, and it prepares to receive friction; if you adjust over it with fire, the gold melts due to the heat, and if you place some sheets in the fire, the craftsman can shape them according to that.
And thus is made the cautery of iron, which you should apply lightly and gradually according to the right procedure.
And I have arranged this chapter on cauteries according to the chapters, and I have placed those for the head among the parts, so that I alleviate the seeker asking for what they desire.
The first chapter.
This cautery is applied to the humidity and coldness that cause headaches and two other conditions resulting from these causes: one is due to soda and a multitude of traumatic fluxes called herpes, affecting the head and parts around the ears with fluid discharge, excessive sleepiness, toothache, and throat pain, and generally all diseases caused by coldness such as paralysis, apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The form of this cautery is as follows: first, order the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine for the head cleansing, for three or four nights, according to what you deem necessary based on the strength of the patient, their age, and their usual habits.
And then command him to shave his head with a razor, and then place him seated on a chair and have him rest his hands on his knees.
And then place the palm of your hand above the eyebrows on the shaved part of the nose between the eyes. But when the heat of your finger reaches that spot, mark it with ink, and then apply the olive oil cautery, which has this form:
Then place upon the affected area a poultice made from lint, in such a way that it descends, with which the skin may be gradually compressed, and you should turn it.
Then lift your hand gently and look at the place, and if you see any amount of the head of the bone exposed, as much as the quantity of a grain of corn, then raise your hand, and if not return your hand to that tool, or another like it, if it is cold, until you can see the bone that I have told you about.
Then take a little salt and dissolve it over the area and leave it for three days.
Then place on the wound a poultice soaked in butter and leave it on until the burn scar is destroyed. Then treat the area with a quadruple ointment until it is healed.
And if it is said that when the swelling has subsided, the sooner the bandage is removed, the better and more beneficial it is.
And some of them say that the wound should be cauterized near the bone, and that the cautery should remain until it adheres to some part of the bone.
Then remove what has adhered to the bone, and then treat it.
And others say that with the cautery it should reach to the deepest part, until it presses firmly on the bone, and this is the way to heal slightly: And they believe that from that place emanates the vapor of the head.
And let the wound be treated for a long time until it is healed and has become firm.
I have not seen these two types of cautery used on everyone, but only on some people out of fear; and one should leave that, in my opinion, if it is better for the health. For the head is easily injured by solutions to continuity as we have testified with other members, and especially when the sick person has a delicate nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and with that one can use it for the benefit of health.
Chapter Second.
On Cautery of the Head.
When chronic and long-lasting pain occurs throughout the head and this condition is prolonged in the patient, and one uses plasters, decoctions, capifers (herbal remedies), poultices, cataplasms, eggs, cats, chickens, and similar treatments, especially if it has already been cauterized with a cautery that we have described but to no avail for any of these causes, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution in its nature; and if it is not delicate and feels no great pain, one can then proceed to use another form of cautery after some time. And then cauterize that area above the crown of their head with cautery until the pain is relieved. Cut open the painful area and expose the bone in the quantity we have described, then cauterize that area with a cautery at the back of the head in the place called "vas" — which is the location where the vein is situated. And do not apply it in this way, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will suffer great pain different from the pain of all other cauteries of the head.
And I will describe this cautery in its place, for it is more delicate to cauterize the horns of the head and the occiput than with that which is used to cauterize the middle part of the head; and this is the form of it.
Chapter Three.
On the cautery for recent swelling.
When severe pain occurs in the part of the head, or if there is lingering pain within the skull, it should be evacuated with medicines that cleanse the head, and another treatment should be administered, which I have described for various illnesses; and if this does not benefit, then cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with a sharp burning medicine, or with iron.
But if you use a burning medicine for cautery, take a piece of amber and burn it, removing the ends from both sides. And then you should incise the area of the pain on the temple with a lancet slightly below where it is felt, so that it is hidden.
And then apply pressure to it with bandages for about three hours; after that, loosen them and remove the amber, and leave the wound covered for two or three days; afterwards, place a piece of wood soaked in butter on it until the area becomes warm.
And then cure it with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you want, use those medicines that I have written in the second treatise on burning medicines.
And this is how you perform cautery with iron using this kind of cautery tool: fire for the cautery, and here is its form:
It is called a nail clamp, because its head is shaped like the form of a nail, in which there is some curvature, and at the middle part of it is a small protrusion.
And then place it on the location of the pain, and hold your hand — so that the artery below is not adhered to — and rotate the iron slightly by little movements quickly.
And if the amount that adheres due to the curvature is such that half of it sticks, raise your hand so that the artery below is not adhered to, so that there is no flow of blood.
And then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the location, and leave it for three days; and then put mulberry leaf poultice on it, and afterwards treat with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, perform cautery at the end of the radial bone according to the cautery method, and be careful not to cut the artery in this non-traditional procedure.
Chapter four.
On the cautery of an ancient wound.
When you treat an ulcer as we have said in the previous treatment and in the divisions of diseases, and if it does not improve, and sometimes due to the disease what has been mentioned about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire is insufficient, then you should ignite the radial cautery until it becomes white-hot, and place it along the line where the pain is, the length of which is half a finger's width or so; and then raise your hand once and firmly press it to cut the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to guard against the continuity of the jawbone, which is necessary for chewing, and that the lizard [muscle] not adhere and the nerves which move it, and that it not become spasm.
And if it is about the custody and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is cut, for in its cutting there is fear, and especially for one who does not know what he is doing and is not studious and experienced; and leave the operation to him at the beginning.
And here I will come to the recollection of the regimen of blood flow from an artery according to the usual manner, in its place in this book.
And if you see from this disease that these cauteries are not sufficient and the patient's condition is tolerable, cauterize him at the appropriate location of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you do, administer the cautery which we have said in the chapter on the rupture of an artery with a cautery having two prongs, and this is better cautery and more effective.
Chapter 5.
Of the cautery for ear pains.
When pain in the ear occurs due to friction and is treated with laxative causes and with the other treatment which I have mentioned in the division, and if the pain does not subside, now use the cautery, and this one has the name puncta, of which this is the form:
And bring the puncta close to the fire, and move it around the entire ear as a wheel turns, or in the vicinity of each part if the pain is in that part. Hold the cautery on the rim of the ear gradually and mark the place with it.
And if the cautery is applied to those points all around the ear, and wherever it affects, take care of the places until they heal.
Chapter VI.
On the cautery of the turtle.
The one for which treatment and cautery are not used is only of the types that come from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cautery for the type that arises from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of illness with jerras and capipurgi and guaraguars, and your treatment does not provide comfort, then it is necessary to cauterize the disease with three cauteries: the first at the root of the ear, and the second a little below in the temple area, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And pause your cautery at the opposite part of the diseased area, because softening occurs only in the part that appears healthy.
The method of cautery is that you cauterize there with the cautery on the right side of the extremity of the most prominent ear, and then a little above the horn of the head, and in another temple area, the length of which should be according to the length of your thumb, and then pause your hand with the cautery until half the quantity of the condition is consumed.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is specific for the ulnar area, the form of which is described above. However, more subtly, it is as you see a little; and it is necessary to use a knife instead of excessive roughness a little.
And immediately treat the area with the liquid that was previously prepared until it is healed.
Chapter VII.
On the cautery for ancient apoplexy.
When apoplexy is ancient and you treat it as we have said and do not apply your medication, and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize that area with four cauteries: on top of all the temples of their head; and one cauterization on the middle part of the head, as we have said; and one cauterization in the nape. And the method of the cautery is done as mentioned above.
And it is already cauterized over the mouth of the stomach; and it is better this way; and proceed with its treatment as appropriate.
Chapter VIII.
On the cautery for oblivion performed with flame.
Give the patient large doses and pills at the beginning to purify the mind.
And then shave the patient's head completely and place on their nape a plaster as described in the chapter on plasters.
And apply that for several applications, for this is the method of the cautery. And do this according to the order I have already mentioned.
And thus if healing occurs well through those methods; but if not, then perform three cauteries on the rear part of their head, which extend from the uppermost part of the head down to the part nearest the neck. And place about the width of a finger between each cauterization, and then proceed to treat the cautery as described.
If you want to apply it for the lesser harm, now make use of cautery in its proper place; and if you wish to apply it more strongly, now cauterize over both sides.
Proceed with the treatment until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form given above.
Chapter IX.
On the cautery for parasites and the softening of the entire body.
Proceed as you would in cleansing the head with scissors and water as we have said; then shave the head of the patient, and afterward cauterize it with cautery in its proper place on the head, and with cautery over all parts of the head, and with cautery on the rear part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening the body to treat it strongly and the patient can tolerate such treatment and the illness is severe, now cauterize it with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and finally with one cautery inside until it is consumed by the skin, and raise the heat moderately. Then proceed with the treatment until it is healed, and let this cautery be olive-shaped.
Chapter X.
On the cautery for epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And therefore, you should cleanse the brain of the patient at the beginning with scissors and with the other treatment we have mentioned in the division when the patient is strong and can tolerate the exception of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate the medicines, at that time administer gargles and powders to clean the mouth, before which for two days purify the food.
And then shave the head of the patient, and then cauterize one with a cautery in the middle part of the head according to the previous method, and another cautery at the back of the head, and on each side of the head cauterize as well. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize with the cauteries I have mentioned, having paralysis and relaxation of the body over the cervical vertebrae and the thoracic vertebrae.
And let it be an olive-cautery as was previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then use a gentle cautery according to this method.
Chapter XI.
On Cauterization for Melancholia.
When the causes of melancholia are due to corrupted humidity and phlegm, then cauterize with the cauteries we have mentioned while inducing paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is an excess leaning towards melancholy, and the body of the patient is humid, then give them to drink what purifies their brain according to what was mentioned earlier in the division.
And then shave the head of the patient, and subsequently place a well-made tortora over the circle, and then apply it to the middle part of the head.
Let the wound be sixty days old, and let it be treated from all sides.
And then apply a liniment; and it is better to use the grease of sheep. And heat that over the fire with a moderately warm heat, which the patient can tolerate when applied. And then place that in the middle of the patient's head in a circle, and let it be rubbed in.
And do this once a week for the wound, and with another regimen to promote healing internally; or if you prefer, cauterize that with small points according to what is necessary, but hold your hand in such an order: this is a special type of cautery that moistens the brain with equilibrium.
And then apply that powder mixed in grease or chicken fat.
Chapter XII.
On the cautery of water descending in the belly.
When it is declared to you the beginning of water in the belly by the signs that I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient something that purifies their head, and protect them from all humidity.
And have the patient sweat in a warm bath for several days.
And then command the patient to shave their head, and cauterize that in the middle of the head; and then cauterize that with two cauteries on both temples if it is the beginning of water descending into two bellies, or on one side if it is the beginning of water in one belly.
Cut and apply cautery to all the veins and arteries that you leave for the ligature; and place the cauteries at a distance in the region of the temples.
And guard against bleeding when it is cauterized; and if you see any bleeding, stop it with some medicine that is available to you.
And I will come to the knowledge which is in the extraction of arteries and their ligation and caution against bleeding.
And in some cases it is cauterized on the neck below two bones with two cauteries properly applied.
Chapter XIII.
On the cauterization of old wounds.
When tears from the eyes are old and flow continually through veins and arteries which are clearly visible on the head, it is verified that this is due to an excess of cold phlegm. At this time, cauterize in that place with a cautery which we have recounted in the chapter about beginning to draw water into the eyes, and apply one cautery in the middle part of the head, and two cauteries at the back of the neck below the temples, and two cauteries at the back of the neck below the bones. And if you need to add something, then at this time cauterize there across the sole of the eye above the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter XIV.
On Cauterization for Nasal Odor.
When you treat that condition as we have said in the division and it does not improve with your medication, then prepare and give the patient cochineal to drink for three nights; afterward, shave his head, and cauterize there a medium-sized cautery with an olive-shaped cautery. And afterward, cauterize there with a carnation-shaped cautery and apply two cauteries above the eyebrows behind the hair, a little bit.
And be careful of the artery so that you do not burn it.
And this is the shape of the carnation-shaped cautery.
Chapter XV.
On Cauterization for Softening the Eyelids.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened due to illness or moisture, then cauterize that eyelid with one cautery in this lunar-shaped cautery; and this is the shape of it:
And if you, when cauterizing above the two eyebrows, use two cauteries throughout, and let them extend towards the temples. And let the length of each cauterization be according to the quantity of the eyebrow.
And do not press too hard with your hand upon the cauteries, but according to the amount by which the third part of the eyelid is adhered.
Chapter XVI.
On cauterizing the eyelid when the hairs turn inward and pinch the eye.
When the hairs of the eyelid turn inward and pinch the eye, cauterization should be done in two ways: either by performing it or using a burning medicine.
If you cauterize with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to trim their hairs as do those who are clean-shaven, such that they are lengthened and even; and if they pinch where they grow, then bandage the eyes so that nothing enters until the hairs have grown back.
When they have grown back and are even, then place the patient's head in your lap, and gently press on the eyelids where you intend to cauterize, marking it out like a leaf of lettuce, with the beginning of the mark near the hairs.
And then place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or mucilage of starch on the eyelid.
And then ignite the cautery which has this shape.
And then cauterize on the figure which you have marked, little by little, in several stages, until the surface of the bone which is like a myrtle leaf is entirely consumed properly.
And the sign of health in this work is that you see the eyelid already contracted and the hairs on it raised due to the puncture of the eye.
And then raise your hand and lay it aside for three days, and afterwards pause on the wound's edge until the scar is healed. And then take care of that wound until it is healed.
But if the hairs around it grow back and the eyelid becomes softened, then perform the cautery on that area again just as you did initially.
But if the hairs remain in the eyelid below, then cauterize that area until it is raised to its natural position and healed so that the hairs do not touch the eye.
But the cautery with a burning medicine is such that you instruct the patient to stretch out the hairs until they are lengthened and healed, and then make from paper or cloth the shape of a myrtle leaf.
And then take known soap and one uncia or more of a living cause, and mix those causes well, and apply this quickly so that it is not rubbed; and spread this on the paper which is made like the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place that over both eyes; and place below the eye wound wrapped in white cloth, and the head of the patient should be at your side.
Here is the translation:
And so when he perseveres in what the swelling of the bite is, then leave off the medicine and bathe it with that which you said; and when the biting ceases, then again apply the medicine and wash the wound with water; and then consider whether the eyelid is raised as much as it was washed, loosen the ligature by fire or incision, for then it is well; and if not return to that of the medicine on the place where you did not use the medicine and let it not darken until your operation be equalled and the wound constricted.
And then pause upon the cut at the border until the root of the injury is eradicated, and then treat that with definite definition or otherwise until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you to guard with vigilant diligence that no rust of the medicine falls into the wound, and if after some days the eyelid becomes softened due to some cause of hair growth in the wound, then repeat your medicine on that place where the eyelid is softened, and properly as you did at the beginning, and then wait until it is healed.
And this is the form of the leaf of myrtle.
And know that the wounds of men vary in size and smallness, and therefore according to this is made your contraction.
And the way to remedy will not be hidden from one who studies in this art.
Chapter XVII.
On the cautery of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the wound.
When you treat fistula with the water we have mentioned in the divisions of diseases and do not confine your treatment, then it is necessary that you cauterize it according to this method: command the patient to place his head or chin on something steady, and hold his head still by someone else's hand in such a way that it is neither too tight nor agitated.
And place cotton or lint on the wound or over the hair of it.
And then heat the cautery, which has this form:
which should be hollow, as in the shape of a reed or eagle's feather, with one end through which the cautery is made.
And if you wish, it should penetrate into the other end. And if you want it to be as thin as a thread.
But the one that is concave is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the putrefaction from it.
And then place the cautery over it, which should be well heated, and hold it from that point with your hand a little away from the wound, so that you do not miss your aim or disturb the patient, and then place the cautery on the edge of the wound and burn it.
And if at the beginning of your cauterization it penetrates to the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization once or twice if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place cotton around it and cover it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
If after eleven days it has not healed, then apply acute corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, according to what is convenient in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which the ancients first described: enter into the location of the fistula and apply to it in that fissure finely powdered material, whose form corresponds to its shape. And pour onto it one ounce of melted lead; with your hand well restrained, so that no injury occurs because the melted lead does not reach the flesh of that area; and it is necessary to place over the wound cloth dipped in egg white or in water, for the lead adheres to the location of the fistula and heals it miraculously.
And if the fistula is healed by what we have said about cauterization and treatment, it is good; but if not, it is necessary to administer traction through the nose and reduce the fistula to the course of the nose as it will come into its proper place.
Chapter XVIII.
On the cauterization of clefts in the teeth.

Chapter XIX.
On cauterizing a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula or on the palate or at the roots of the teeth there is an abscess and it becomes inflamed, and then it breaks open, and then it becomes old, and flows continuously and a fistula is formed; when you treat that and it does not improve, now is the time to cauterize according to the quantity received in the fistula, and then place the heated cautery into the tract of the fistula, and with your hand ensure that the hot iron reaches the bottom of it; at the end do this once or twice. Then treat what we have said about internal treatment until it is healed.
And if the fistula is severed and it heals, that is good; but if not, now it is necessary to discover the location and excise the corrupted bone as will be described in its chapter.
Chapter XX.
On cauterizing the teeth and softening the gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are decayed, and you treat them with medicines but they do not improve, now place the tip of the cautery in your own mouth, and then ignite the cautery whose form will be described later; then place the tube over the tooth and insert the heated cautery quickly into it, holding it firmly so that the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
Then take your hand, and then repeat the cauteries as many times as you wish.
And then the patient should rinse his mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the decayed tooth is hardened and the softened gum is strengthened and the corrupted moisture is dried up.
Chapter XXI.
On the cautery for toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is pain or inflammation, and if you do not wish to treat it with medicine, then cautery in that case is appropriate in two ways: either with a cow's horn or with fire.
Cautery with a horn is when you take a cow's horn and heat it over fire, then cut cloth and wrap it around the end of the horn. Then immerse the heated horn in water quickly before placing it on the painful tooth while it is still warm.
And then repeat that several times until the effect of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or cloth in the aforementioned horn, and place it on the painful tooth, then place heated iron on top of it until the heat reaches the source of the pain in the tooth.
However, cautery with fire is that you attend to the thickness of the tube of iron or copper, so that in its middle there is some thickness and the intensity of the heat does not reach the mouth of the patient.
From the fire make the cautery of which I will describe the form, and place it upon the tooth and hold it there with your hand until the cautery is rubbed in; and do this several times so that the pain may be alleviated either on that day or on the following day.
And it is necessary that by pressure of that cautery the patient fill his mouth with hot water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize as much as you wish according to what is possible for you.
And this is the form of the cannula:
Chapter XXII.

And then care for it with dressings and bandages until it is healed.

Chapter XXVII.
On cauterization of an inflamed bladder.

Chapter XXVIII.
On the Incision of an Abscess of the Testicle with Caustics.
When you find an inflammation or abscess in the testicle and want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the testicle or in the tunica, then if it is in the flesh of the testicle, the patient will complain of heaviness and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the tunica of the testicle, then the pain will be sharp and often it exhausts the skills of the physicians to treat it.
Therefore, the patient should be positioned on his back; then feel the location of the abscess carefully; and subsequently apply the cautery which resembles a reed, the form of which was described on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until the entire area is consumed, until it reaches the cicatrix, until it becomes dry and heals.
And then treat that abscess like the treatment of other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not appropriate to use this form of cautery except by one whose study in the art of medicine is extensive and who has known many diseases; but even then, this experiment should be attempted only a few times on such an operation, but rather leave that to us, and it is better.

Chapter XXX.

And then take care of the cautery so that it does not shake for too many days, for it is more convenient than other causes previously mentioned in the treatment.

And let him be restricted from work and overeating and drinking, and speaking loudly, for when he follows this regimen of complete healing, he will be healed.
And I will speak of the cure of ruptures in other parts of the belly, and so at the beginning, if you do not want them to remain, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let that which is excised from it be as thick as one third of it; and thereafter treat it as we have said, and it will not require adjustment.
And some earlier ones said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery according to this form, as it is marked by a template:
and a line is marked where you will proceed latitudinally in the skin above the rupture, and another line above it; then mark your hand with a nail-shaped cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter XLVI.
On cauterization of torsion.
When pain occurs in any member due to falling or striking and that pain persists for a long time and is not relieved by medicine or cure, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be applied to every member according to its size and smallness, its strength and weakness, the location of the torsion, and the site of the pain.
If he is healed by the first cautery, that is good; but if not, then apply the cautery again. For it is customary with such pains that they are points of the member nearest to them, so you must achieve these points with the cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter XLVII.
On cauterization of leprosy.
Some cases of leprosy are greatly helped by cautery, but rarely when the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of flesh and melancholy.
And so when you wish to cauterize that patient, consider whether the leprosy is commissional and has been partially cured by what we have said in the division and does not continue, and if you fear for the patient that the corruption may encompass the whole body and its entirety, then cauterize it over the head with four cauteries: one at the middle of the head, and the second further down near the forehead; both on two temples; and one at the back, above the neck or nape.
And finally with the cauteries, apply them slightly enough to create a small impression on the bone, so that the scales are displaced and allow the expulsion of thick vapors; and cauterize over the wound as needed.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the body and is clearly visible, then it is necessary that you cauterize those areas with the cauteries which we have mentioned at the beginning; one cauterization on the tip of the nose, two on each cheek, two on each side of the neck vertebrae, six on the lower back vertebrae, one large cauterization near the buttocks, one on each buttock, two on each thigh, one on each calf, two on each ankle and one on each heel, and two on each foot arch. Cauterize those areas over all the joints of the mentioned limbs and feet, and on each sole of their feet, and on the hollows of their hands.
And be careful of the nerves that run over the heel so that you do not burn them.
And in some cases, it is cauterized with one cautery on the bone of the little finger and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the spleen.
And know that the more you apply cauteries, the more beneficial and effective they are; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery as others do, because his body is already numbed.
And it is necessary that the cauteries be large or few according to the limbs and joints, depending on the type of tools used.
And apply the cautery with flour of fried chickpeas mixed with honey, and continue the treatment until it is healed.
Chapter XLVIII.
On cauterization for fainting.
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and does not heal, then you should cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the magnitude of the limb or the smallness of it.
And let the fire for the cautery enter slightly under the surface of the skin. And then treat that with a remedy so that it may be healed.
And sometimes faintness is cauterized by treating certain points, which are mostly in the back and spine, and at the sites of the nerves that govern that limb, and thus the numbness is alleviated.
But do not proceed with this unless you are knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limb and the distribution of the nerves throughout the moving body.
Chapter XLIX.
On cauterization of ulcers that are inflamed.
When an ulcer is old and does not respond to any of the medicinal remedies, then you should cauterize it with a cautery slightly above the surface according to the quantity of the depth under the skin until the whiteness and the color of it change.
And then treat that with flour of eggs and lentils in rose oil and leaves of arnica, and the blood of dove or the blood of quail, taking an equal part of each and mixing them all together, and then apply a cloth soaked in this mixture until it is healed.
Chapter I.
On cauterizing wounds.
When cancer begins and you want to remove it, then cauterize that part with a cauterizing agent in the form of a circle around it as it appears.
And the wise ones say that it should be cauterized with the final cautery in its proper place; but I have not seen this done, for I fear that it may become ulcerated, and I have observed this in some cases.
It is more correct to cauterize around the part in a circular manner as we have said, or rather as described.
Chapter II.
On cauterizing abscesses.
When an abscess is delayed in maturing, and this is due to the excessiveness of its formation or the condition of the patient, who is old and debilitated with little blood, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the maturation of the abscess; then cauterize around it with small punctures using a cautery instrument; and immediately apply something that will help it mature more quickly.
But if you wish to puncture it with a cautery, then choose the cautery that has this shape; and place it in the proper location of the abscess until it penetrates the core.
And if the cautery is applied to a location that is further away to spare the tissue of the arm; and then care for it until it heals.
Chapter LII.
On cauterizing with corrosives.
A corrosive is a wandering corruption in the limb, which eats away just as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, when you see the corrosive in a place where it can withstand cautery with fire, then you ignite and cauterize small spots, and larger ones as is appropriate in the location where the corrosion is.
And proceed to cauterize it thoroughly until you eradicate all the corrosion and leave no cause for it to remain.
And then dress it for three days, and apply ointment mixed with oil to the cauterized spots three times a day until all the scars are eradicated and all the corruption is gone.
And then treat it with substances that promote flesh growth.
But if after three days you see that healthy flesh is growing where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cautery on what remains in the corrupted areas.
And some corrosions are treated with sharp medicine, for that is the method instead of cautery. But cautery with fire is quicker to provide relief.
And I have already described the treatment with sharp medicine in the division above, so use that when you deem it necessary.
Chapter LIII.
On cauterizing of nails and other similar cases.
In many cases, when dealing with the parts of the foot affected by severe illness called gout, which is caused by thick and swollen matter, you should cauterize it; there are two ways to do this, one is cauterization with fire and the other is cauterization with hot water.
Cauterization with fire is done by igniting the cauterizing instrument shaped like a half moon, which should be made of iron according to the quantity that will contain the thick matter from all parts, and it should have fine edges.
And then place the ignited instrument over the thick matter, and then move your hand with the cauterizing instrument around the thick matter until the cauterization reaches the depth of the thick matter. And let that remain for three days until it begins to discharge pus. And then apply a plaster made of crushed wild mugwort and salt to it, and leave the plaster on for one night, for this will help eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a healing ointment for generating flesh until it is healed.
And if it does not convert, and in many cases it occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, you should administer cauterization with hot water. Take a small tube made of iron or other material shaped like a half moon, and place it over the thick matter; then pour a little hot water into the tube and hold it with your hand while rotating it completely, pressing the edge of the tube against the root of the thick matter so that the hot water flows along the root of the thick matter. Wait for a short time after the application of the hot water for about an hour, and then remove it, as this will help eradicate the thick matter with its roots.
And thus you should approach each one that you may come upon all of which naturally in the body.
And approach the care of those pus that the roots of the nails are eradicated with those remedies which the flesh generates.
Chapter LIII.
On the cautery for trembling.
When you encounter any trembling due to irritation in the nerves or from fever causing irritation, or from some similar cause, it is then appropriate that you cauterize it with four cauteries or five on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra with one cauterization; and at the feet of the patient, and above the stomach, use an olive-shaped cauterization.
And the trembling of the patient subsides and the health hastens to overcome the illness.
Chapter LIV.
On the cautery for accidental injuries to the body.
When in the body there appear accidental swellings and sores that are caused by thick, corrupted matter; therefore, at the beginning when they appear, it is appropriate to be cauterized over the entire head of the fistula with a fine cautery made from myrtle wood, the tip of which is extended over fire; or with the long thorns of the erestologia plant; or with a lens-shaped cautery.
And sometimes the cautery is also applied at the beginning of its expulsion according to this method; and if it does not occur as expected, the excess is disrupted by doing so, and heals from it.
However, it must be done near evacuation or phlebotomy.
Chapter LVI.
On cauterization of blood flow caused by an accidental cut to an artery.
If the flow of blood from an artery that has been cut is due to the disease that caused it before, or due to the beginning of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similar cases, then it is difficult to remedy that.
When such a thing happens, quickly take your finger and place it over the opening of the artery, and press on it so tightly that the blood flow stops below your finger, and does not escape.
And then place in the fire some small and large cauteries made of olive wood, many of them, and blow on them until they are lit.
And then take one of those cauteries, either small or large depending on the location and disease in which the artery is ruptured, and place it quickly after you have removed your finger, so that the blood is sealed and restricted.
And if the blood is expelled from the opening of the artery when you remove your finger and extract the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from those that are prepared in the fire, and continue doing this one after another until the blood is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and make sure it does not cause other harm.
Know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to restrain the blood at that time, especially if the artery is large, unless you use one of four methods: either use cauterization as mentioned before, or perform ligation when it cannot be extracted, for when it is extracted the extremities beyond it are burned and thus the blood is restrained; or apply a bandage soaked in strong vinegar or some other binding agent; or place them on medicines whose virtue is to constrict the blood; and strengthen this with a pillow and proper constriction.
He who administers proper restraint with bandages and compression using cloths or by placing them in positions away from burning causes and similar risks should do so carefully and slowly.
If one must act and no doctor or medicine is present, then gently press the index finger on the opening of that vein as we have said, and understand the strong constriction inside so that the blood stops flowing from the main part of the wound, and do not move your finger over the artery; and sprinkle cold water over the artery until it becomes congealed and thickened; and then decide whether cauterization or medicine is necessary.

Chapter VII.
On the treatment of ear obstruction.
Sometimes some children are born from their mothers' wombs unperfored, and this also happens in some adults, where there is an obstruction in the ear canal due to a growth or flesh; and this obstruction sometimes occurs at the entrance of the ear canal, which cannot be seen clearly, and sometimes it is located outside.
And that which occurs at the entrance of the ear canal, which cannot be seen clearly, is in the majority of cases difficult to treat.
And in that case where you can see clearly, it is necessary to expose the ear of the patient to the sun and observe it. And if you see an obstruction clearly, open it with a fine spatula of this form:
If the tip of it where it is narrow, with one side sharp and the other not; and the spatula should be double-edged so as not to cause damage to the ear.
And if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or in the canal, then use fine forceps and carefully cut away all the flesh within.
And if there is an obstruction in the ear canal, then take a thin reed and heat it slightly over fire, and then insert it into the ear.
And if you feel an obstruction with what the patient finds easy to reach due to difficulty in hearing, try to force the obstruction out with what is easily accessible; and be careful not to damage the nerves of the ear.
And then place in the ear a piece of cloth dipped in the quantity appropriate to the size of the wound, which you have wrapped in Egyptian cotton; and do this for several days until you are sure of the consolidation of the injury; or take something that can be soaked in water and powdered with finely ground herbs and use it; and if you see the ear already inflamed, then remove what was there and replace it with something wrapped in wax mixed with rose oil, until the inflammation subsides; and afterward treat it until it is healed.
But if there is bleeding, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use another treatment that stops the flow of blood, which is described in the chapter on distillation.
Chapter VIII.
On the treatment of boils that occur on the eyelids.
Boils that occur on the eyelids are sometimes moist, and at other times they are dry.
Therefore, you should have those (boils) ready in small containers or jars, and then incise them with the edge of their roots, and afterward apply finely powdered herbs to the areas.
If there is pain and a flow of blood from the veins, then at that hour you should cauterize those places which are suitable for cauterization under the clavicle.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions and wounds return when there remains some cause for them; and when they are cauterized with fire, those causes are consumed and do not return.
Chapter IX.
Treatment for an ailment that affects the eyelids.
Wounds that remain in the eyelids due to some cause similar to a severe condition in its strength and hardness, and for this reason it is called severe.
And it is an aggregation of thick moisture in the eyelid both above and below.
Therefore, the treatment for this is to consider at that moment whether the severity arises from what appears on the surface of the eyelid and affects it entirely; and the cause of this is slight.
Therefore, apply something over that area along the length of the fissure, and then carefully remove it entirely with ease until it is evacuated, and then suspend it with an ointment and bandage it.
And if the condition is severe, then you should adjust it with stitches; and take care of it until it is healed.
And if it is minor, no harm will come to you from that because the warmth of treatment restores and heals it.
And if the swelling is inclining towards the inner parts of the eyelid, you should lift the surface inwardly so that the eyelid suspends the swelling and presses it from all sides; and if the eyelid because of this incision is stretched out, do not attribute this to any cause.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with something that heals so that it may be healed.
Chapter 10.
About the treatment around the eye above the wound.
Axcirnath is a condition that arises in the layers of the eyelid above, and it occurs more commonly in children. It aggravates their wounds and causes them to suffer from catarrh due to death; they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because tears continuously collect in their eyes.
And because of this, you see them pressing their faces all the time, and they sleep with their faces pressed down; and above their lower eyelids are wet eyebrows, which have been covered by inflammation, and the impending formation of acirnath is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel acirnath within them.
And there is a method of operation for when the patient rests their head in your lap, and you place a cloth over them and make a circle according to the quantity that contains arthernach from all sides, and then place it on that area and press with your fingers all around so that acernath aggregates in the middle of the circle, and then introduce moisture into the opening using spatum incident along the latitude, and the incision should not be larger than the incision made in flavotomy.
In the middle of the incision which you make in the bone, proceed until you reach the marrow, for in major dislocations you will find marrow by means of a fracture according to the form of white fat.
Then extract that which you wrapped in the cloths between your said parts and roll it between your right and left hands until it is dispersed.
And be careful not to probe too deeply into the fracture so that you do not reach the brain, and avoid doing so.
But if the blood does not appear to you at the beginning of the fracture, then probe gently into the fracture until you encounter blood; and then extract it as we have said; and subsequently dip cloths in warm water and vinegar and place them over the area and sprinkle with powder.
And some people sprinkle salt and place it in the concavity of the fracture so that what remains will absorb this humidity.
And then take care of it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs in the area, then treat it with soft plasters until it is healed.
Chapter XI.
On the methods of examining the brain.
When there are white patches among the eyelashes that grow below the natural lashes and become old, these affect the brain and cause various illnesses such as continuous tears and softening of the eyelids and membrane, which can lead to brain damage known as "tactinir del huel." Treatment is done in four ways: either by cauterization with fire; or using sharp medicine as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cauterization; or it is performed through incision and suturing; or by means of tubes, as I will explain.
Therefore, when you place the head of the patient in such a position; turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it turns well, that is good; but if not, insert a needle into the one which contains pus below the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate to the pus from above, and this should be — near the edge of the eyelid where hair grows — near the hair, and draw out the pus to the parts above with the eyelid, and turn that part with the ray.
And then probe in the part inside of the eyelid below the hair towards the spatum incident to the lacrimal major into the lacrimal minor; and then extract the pus and place below the eyelid small particles of wood or cloth, and then bandage over the eyelid with a piece of cloth, and it should be like the figure of a leaf of myrtle.
However, it must be the figure according to the quantity of elevation of the eyelid, for this already varies among people, since there are those whom you need to incise from the eyelid a great quantity according to the amount by which the eyelid is softened.
And then probe with the probe on both lines which you have marked, and begin from the lacrimal major into the lacrimal minor. And if your fissure near the natural hair is as thick as that of the king.
And then place the incision at one of the two angles of the chain, and then excoriate that completely, and then apply to the wound both lips with wool grease, and wipe away the blood, and make adhere what is superfluous of the pus on two eyebrows with some viscous causes, if you wish to do this, and otherwise, do not worry.
About four days after sewing up the intestines, start the cure with this liquid.
And if you wish, treat the wound without suturing it, and cure that with a liquid that dries and contracts, since the eyelid is elevated due to the sealing of the wound and the accumulation of pus; but suturing is better. This is what the ancients recommended.
However, let there be careful attention regarding the condition, and it is of good practice, and there should not be fear in it.

Chapter XII.
On Removing Stinging Hairs from the Eye with a Needle When the Hair is Firmly Embedded.
When there are unnatural hairs, hard and rough, that sting the eyes, then the procedure is that you take a fine needle and place it in the hair root finely and gently, and then adjust the ends of it carefully and hold each one in a very subtle manner. And let the length of the threads be equal to the quantity of one palm.
And then prepare another fine thread below it briefly, and use the ends of that one if you wish.
And then place the malice of its head in your hand, and do it near the sun so that your work may be clear, for this is a subtle and intricate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the hair that needs to be transplanted, and make it penetrate quickly into the eyelid before you remove it from above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair in from above and insert it into the recipient site, and let there be one or two or three hairs and no more.
And then pull your hand gently so that the hair is moved into place on the recipient site on the eyelid with the natural hair.
But if it is pulled on the recipient site and the hair does not move into place, pull the recipient site towards the thread that you have prepared with it, so that it moves into place from the excess trauma.
And look at it with the eye, for now you will pull the recipient site and the thread, and tie each one securely, and leave the ties for two or three days until the hair adheres to its place and grows on that good flesh.
And if the hair is shorter, now you should take one long hair from among the natural hairs and place it there, or leave it so that it grows in some space near by, and then raise it.
Chapter XIII.
On the treatment of an abscess that occurs in the upper eyelid.
When an abscess occurs in the eye, and it is contracted when the eyelid is closed, either to the right or left side, due to poor healing of wounds or incisions or cauterizations, it is called leporine. This kind of abscess can be either natural or accidental, caused by poor healing of wounds, incisions, or cauterizations, or similar malpractices.
The method of the operation is such that you should cut open that consolidation and separate its lips, then place between them a piece of linen cloth, and bind it there until it heals.
And it is not advisable to use treatments for it that dry out or constrain it, because if you do this, the abscess will return with pus worse than before, no matter how much time passes.
Use treatments that soften it, such as fenugreek, and apply the water in which its leaves and seeds have been boiled, or juice from dill, which should be mixed with some oil to make it liquid, then immerse the cloth in this mixture and use it for treatment.
The treasure of your treatment is that you should puncture any part that has not yet healed according to the form it had at the beginning.
And this operation that we have described is nothing but a correction of an abscess that does not return to its original form as it was before.
Chapter XIV.
Of the treatment of ehacrati which is done on the eyelid below.
This ehacrati which is done on the lower eyelid, which is properly named ehacrati; and it can be either natural or accidental.
The accidental one is caused by a plague or a tear or cauterization or other similar reasons.
And the way of the operation is that you take a needle with which to make a double thread, and place it in the flesh, and pass it from the left lacrimal bone to the right lacrimal bone, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
And then stretch the flesh in the parts above with the needle and cut along with the needle using a broad spatula. And then if you return the shape of the eyelid as needed, it is good; and if not, take a rod and place it in the incised figure, and use that to convert the eyelid, and close both openings on the side of the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two openings be at two corners of the incision you have made where they meet, and let their corner form a shape when they come together.
And this figure is the Greek letter: lambda.
And then remove the flesh in the quantity of its width below, what shrinks the eyelid.
And then adjust the separated parts and sew them together with linen thread; and then treat those as we have said regarding softening medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if through excision, incision, or cautery you have reached the place where a fissure, suture, or cauterization is needed, then you should make a flat incision above the eyelashes as has been demonstrated previously. And then separate the membrane between the two lips as we have said.
In the summary of the speech on the treatment of excisions when they are above or below, let it proceed according to the operation, as it is prepared in the form of an excision, and that one is in the form of various shapes.
And the skilled practitioners always prepare in every possible way within themselves to return the figure to its natural form or close to it, and that patient according to the convenience of the operation and the instruments in the major dispositions.
Chapter XV.
On the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation occurs in certain individuals such that the eyelid is adhered above to the sclera, and its movement and natural functions are restricted, then you should introduce a probe below the eyelid, elevate it with it, or extend it to the root.
And then you should incise the adherence with a fine probe, which should not be sharp at its tip, holding it bluntly so that the patient is not disturbed by the operation, and move the probe gently so as not to damage the eye with its sharpness.
And your incision should be such that it easily separates the adherence, allowing the eyelid to return to its natural form, and ensuring that the entire adherence is healed.
And you should put in the wound salt water with which you wash; or distill in that of rose dissolved in water.
And then separate between the eyelid and the wound with a clean instrument, and place on the wound linen infused in egg white, and after the third day use consolidating treatments until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
On the incision of the nail and on the swelling of the flesh around the tear ducts.

And so you should follow with the skin to the rear part of it.

Chapter XVII.
On the incision of styes and of what is born from flesh tightly attached to the eye.
In some cases on the eyes of some people, flesh grows, and it is raised until it covers the pupil, or approaches or stretches over the eyelids and severely pulls the eyelids apart in various parts and is assembled like a small turret.
The operation there is that the patient places his head in your lap; then open the eyes of that person and collect all that flesh at once appropriately, and with this use forceps or tweezers; then incise the flesh at the beginning until it is completely finished with the scissors, and be careful not to scratch the eye during your operation.
And your incision should be with the scalpel we have recommended for nail incisions, or with small tweezers with which hair is plucked, depending on the form that will come next according to this chapter.

And if any cause arises to guard against bleeding and the wound is in danger of becoming cauterized, and you fear an abscess from cautery, then leave the wound open and treat that abscess with whatever remedy will cure it, and subsequently proceed with the operation once again as soon as it has healed, and similarly handle the flesh around this type of wound.
However, you should be cautious about removing all healing from the wound naturally or accidentally; use tools of iron, and do not place your hand in any of these operations until you see that the member can withstand the operation.

Chapter XX.
On the reduction of the prominence of the eye.

Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of flesh wounds on the nose.
When on the nose there arise various flesh growths that are attached, among which there is one cause that resembles a spider web of heavy threads, or a water polyp; and this illness is called polypus because it resembles such a water creature. Therefore, for the one who has these flesh growths that are not fraudulent or cancerous, it is advisable to make the patient sit between your hands and place it in the sun, and starve the nostrils of them, and apply poultices occasionally to those flesh growths. If the moisture does not penetrate as needed, then you will know that deep within there is flesh that has remained on the surplus bones of the nose to which incision has not reached.

Chapter XXV.
About the veins in the extremities of the nose.
Most bruises occur in the extremities of the nose, and they are magnified and treated within a few days until such time as it is seen fit to attend to them.
And for this reason, you should address it at the beginning of its appearance and treat all of it. And then focus on the place where you cauterize or use medicinal remedies that burn, which are applied there.
But if the treatment is delayed until it has become magnified, then consider whether it is hardened, firm, dark in color, and insensitive. At this point, do not touch it with an iron instrument, as doing so might cause severe damage to the one performing the procedure; for many injuries occur due to such careless handling of the condition.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch and not dark in color, and it is possible to treat it at any location without fear, then evacuate the pus and incise it without hesitation, and care for the area with remedies that dry and heal until it is fully healed.
Chapter XXVI.
On sewing the nose and lips and ear when the continuity is disrupted by plague or similar causes.
Know that when a disruption in continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, few people are skilled in such an operation.
Therefore, when dealing with any cause of this nature, you should consider whether it is a fresh wound or due to blood loss. Then, you should approximate the two edges of the wound with stitches and subsequently care for it until it heals.
And if the lips of a wound of continuity are already separated and each one is made whole, now you must scrape each part of its apparent edge until it becomes clean. And then adjust the two lips with sutures and strengthen them, and over them sprinkle powder of dragon’s blood and thrice daily apply a plaster of mastic or some other consolidating plaster. Let that lie for two days or three, and then remove it and change the plaster until the pus has ceased to discharge, and then treat with an ointment until it is healed.
The method of the suture is that you add the wound of continuity either with needles as we have recommended for the suture of the abdomen, or you may join the wound of continuity with thread as you see fit.
Chapter XXVII.
On the extraction of those causes that appear in the two lips in their inner parts.
Often in some men in the inner parts of their lips there appear certain hard abscesses resembling flesh of mice, and some of them are smaller and some larger.
Therefore, you must turn the lips and cut open each of those hardnesses from all sides, and then when you have made an incision, fill the place with finely shredded wool until the blood is constricted, and then wash it with vinegar and treat the area with something astringent until it is healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the incision of flesh attached to the gums.
In many cases there appears on the gum flesh attached to which the ancients gave the name albulus; then you should suspend that part with a sponge or have in your hand a sponge and incise that area down to its base, and allow the pus to run out or the blood.
And then apply to the wound powder that is astringent or excitative.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it again secondarily, and cauterize it, for after the cauterization it will not return.
Chapter XXIX.
On the extraction of teeth with forceps.
When on the surface of the teeth of both jaws and between the gums closely attached there are rough, sharp edges, lumps, and some areas are blackened and appear green or yellow, causing corruption to the gum and making the teeth lumpy.
And therefore you should make the patient sit between your hands and place his head on your knees, and extract the teeth in which such rough edges appear and cause grittiness so that none of them remain, and this will remove the blackness and greenish discoloration and other issues so that they are cleansed.
If this can be done at the beginning of the extraction, that is good; but if not, then you should repeat the extraction on those teeth another day and on the second and third days until it reaches the final stage as you wish.
And know that teeth have need of various tools of different forms and shapes according to what is prepared for your operation. Chapter XXX.
On the extraction of teeth.
When you treat the tooth of its pain with all gentleness and delay in total extraction, for there is no remedy that can replace the root once it is extracted, since it is a substance sublime within which makes excuse for the extraction of it.
But when the necessity forces you to extract that tooth, you must firmly be certain which is the painful tooth, for often the pain deceives and leads one to believe it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, but then the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, as we have already seen through the operations of surgeons.
Therefore, you must be certain which is the painful tooth. Once you know well, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with spatula in which there is some virtue until it dissolves the tooth from the gum entirely, and then take it out using your fingers or with fine forceps little by little until it comes out.
And then hold firmly with strong fingers for good traction or firm grasp, and place the head of the patient between your hands so that he is well held without discomfort, and then extract the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause greater pain to the patient.
And so if it is not dislodged, then take one of those instruments and place it there from all sides with ease and exert effort to remove it just as it was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is loose or corroded, then you should wrap that part in cloth and secure it with a thin thread so that it does not break during your compression on that side holding it.
And you should act carefully during the incision around all parts of the gum and guard with your strength that you do not break or damage it, and return to the problem for the sake of improving it so that it is better than the initial pain.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their hasty and daring attempts at extraction without administering what we have recommended; for many times this leads to serious conditions in patients, which are much harder to treat, and the roots of them become completely or partially destroyed, or you may extract it along with some bones of the mandible as many times has been testified.
And after the extraction of it, the mouth should be washed with cold water or warm salt water.
And if there is bleeding due to this, as it often happens, then take some quantity of sugar and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that does not suffice, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and subtle holders with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should be long in length, short in handle, and thick, so that they do not bend when you need to operate with them.
Let them be such as you use for rough handling when straining them, that they may not be folded, but let their extremities be short.
Let them be made of Indian iron, tapering towards the ends, and at the ends let there be teeth which enter into each other and strain by strong compression, and sometimes the ends of these should have the shape of a bird’s beak called ciconia, that is, a heron, and they should be very strong for constriction.
