And know that the bones in those who are old and in young children, as well as in the elderly, cannot be set or consolidated according to their nature by the drying of the members.
However, nature adjusts and applies them over the broken bone from all parts for some cause similar to glue, in which there is a thickness that adheres and strengthens it as they congeal together until the end of its strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning does not prevent it from performing its work.
And for this reason it is necessary to avoid giving the patient foods that are heavy and viscous and gross, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for the fracture of the bone of the chest is different from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is different from the fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all other members are different among themselves.
And you will find a description of every type of fracture, expanded in its own separate chapter.
Consider the diversity of fractures within themselves. And the types of bone fractures are diverse within themselves; for there is one type of curved fracture different from those that occur in the stars; and some fractures are made according to the length of the bone: and among the fractures of the shaft and splinters and complete and incomplete, just as it is done when a pen is prepared for writing, there is a fracture due to impact and disruption in the head, and there is a small crack fracture.
And each type has its own proper treatment in the restoration of it, as will be recalled separately in their respective places.
Consider the signs of a bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: its twisting, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and what happens when you press on it with your hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the location or sound, and when you touch the bone with pressure and the patient does not feel great pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there may be twisting or a small crack. In this case, do not press on it with your hand and compress it entirely; instead, wait and apply the medicines of which mention will be made shortly at the moment when it is appropriate to do so; and it may also be narrow due to subtle constriction.
And know that when the place is arched and not divided into two or another part where it does not suffer from swelling, you should begin from your hour to correct the condition of the affected area where an abscess occurs.
And if an abscess occurs there, then leave it for some days until the abscess subsides; and afterwards bandage it according to each method that you can of lightness and of support.
And know that the restoration of it and the alignment of it is easier than with the bone in which swelling has occurred.
And afterwards strengthen it according to what will come to mind regarding it.
But if the bone is swollen, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from both parts, whether it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords or one cord and your hand.
And place yourself in such a position that you can put the limb in a bandage according to its natural figure, while the parts of the broken member are extended, then it forces you to return those addictions with support and according to each way that you can in their places.
And be careful according to your strength that no harm comes from your operation either by injury or damage; and strive according to your ability to apply one bone to its companion bone according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that opportune moment you touch with your hand that which needs attention, and if you see any diverse cause, correct it and bandage it according to the extent of your skill.
And from an incorrect and excessive extension or compression, so do false physicians cause an abscess to occur through their operation, or they cause a contraction in the limb as we have seen this happen due to their improper manipulations.
And the quality of the restriction of the tranquil limb is what I recommend; because you should know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and greatness and in the form of each one.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and similar ones, the wrapping should be of soft fine cloth.
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or the chest, it is necessary to wrap them with hard bandages, because these bandages connect and secure the large limb firmly from all sides in an equal restriction, so that no dirt enters.
And when you begin, after the proper equalization of the limb, pause conveniently for that, on the site of the fracture with three or four layers of bandages depending on the limb; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And apply to it the most sovereign poultice for the place of the fracture, and tighten moderately that bandage with which you have bound the place of the fracture. And then lengthen the bandage around the place of the fracture a little, and loosen the binding gradually, until you take off most of it.
And then take another bandage and wrap it around the broken place with a binding, and afterwards apply to it the bindings below the fracture; and let your operation be in the tightening of the binding and in the loosening of that one as we have said about the first binding above.
And then place between the bandages a piece of soft linen or cloth on which the unevenness of the fracture is padded, if there is any unevenness; and if not, do not place anything there for this reason.
And then place on the other bandage, and afterwards apply plasters properly made soon if there is no inflammation or abscess in the area, and now place on that which cures the abscess and reduces the swelling; and leave this for several days; and then you tighten over it the bandages.
And let the plasters be half a cubit wide in length, and well formed wisely; or let them be made of willow wood; or from strips of palm; and let the width of each plaster be according to this form and this figure:
However, the plaster that is placed over the fracture should be a little thicker and longer than the others, and the length of the plaster according to how it fits the limb in size and smallness; and then tighten over the plasters another bandage as your first binding; and afterwards bind above with wax moderately as we have said about binding, and it is that your binding should be tighter near the fracture, and as much as it is distant from the fracture, the binding should be looser.
And let the threads be medium in thickness and fineness, and let them be soft; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen some foolish people use ropes of twisted hemp for their threads, it is a great mistake, when one considers the equality of tension.
And likewise, very fine threads are not appropriate, for you will not achieve the desired tension with them, and it is necessary that there be not less than one digit between knot and knot.
And if the patient is injured at the ends of the bones near the sutures in such places, then pause and use threads of lion’s sinew or matted wool until it does not cause harm to him.
Sometimes a fracture occurs due to a blow, and there is a disruption in the chain, and I recall this particular case in the second chapter.
And know that not all broken members should be set with splints on the first day; the reason for this is that when the member is large, it should not be placed on splints immediately but after about five or seven days or more, according to your certainty of the occurrence of an abscess.
And this is the method of plasters with which the Ancients restored fractures, dislocations, and twists, which were applied over injuries.
Method of a plaster for the quick restoration of fractures, suitable for delicate conditions and especially for women and children, as it does not generate heat or irritation: take flour powder, which is the residue from the grinding process that sticks to the walls of the mill.
And confine it as it is, but sieve it with white cloth, and pause the compounding of it not thin nor subtle; and then use it.
Another method for plasters to restore fractures, dislocations, and twists: recipe of asafetida, of lead danum, of acacia, of inula, and migrauch, which is the rind of wild millet, of each a drachm x; of myrrh and aloes each a drachm v; aloe chil each a drachm xx; all are to be ground and sieved together and mixed with water or with white cloth if the preparation is warm; and then use this plaster, for it is well composed and quickly restores broken bones, and confers health upon men.
Another method for a plaster to treat fractures and twists: recipe of migrauch (rind of wild millet) and met of white lead each a drachm x; of myrrh and aloes each a drachm v; of acacia a drachm vi; of Armenian bole a drachm xx.
All should be triturated in a mortar with a pounding motion, and then sieved, and compounded with water or with white cloth if the mixture is warm, and it should be administered.
Method for a plaster for joints and bones that have come out of their places, and for accidental pain in those areas and for fissures and fractures: take clean wool and immerse it in vinegar and warm oil and apply it to the area.
Note that this plaster is not for restoration, but for abscesses and properly expelling pains.
Another method for a plaster that restores broken bones: take fresh leaves of the fig tree and leaves of wild poppy; crush them together and make a plaster from them.
Method of applying a shortened plaster for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want to resolve the remnant of an abscess: take rhizomes of elder, chamomile flowers, and violets, and flour of spelt, of each one part; mix all finely and cook in wine, if the member is not burned.
But if it is burned, now confect that in cold cilantro water or in water and use it.
Another method of a stronger plaster for resolution than this first one, when there occurs a hard abscess during the restoration of the limb: take rhizomes of elder and seeds of linseed, and buckthorn, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of each one equal part; all should be finely mixed and cooked in savory water or warm water or in wine, whichever is appropriate according to the quality of the limb and the condition indicated by its appearance.
However, Hippocrates does not mention in his book that it should be paused on the broken limb during its restoration other than cerot made of wax and oil, and he recommends that it be midway between thickness and fineness.
However, Galen is seen to have them applied on the broken limb during its restoration and for causes in which there is drying with a little heat as well as substances like myrrh, saffron, and similar to those causes that we have recommended.
Method of plaster to relieve injury and pain: take linseed, sesame seeds, human hair cut or bird's feather, elder rhizome, and salt, in equal parts; mix them finely and sieve them and cook them, then make a plaster.
The amount according to which it is necessary that the dressing remain and should then be removed, is that you consider whether there is no pain or swelling or the bone is not displaced from its place; otherwise, do not remove it for several more days. And if there is severe pain or swelling or inflammation in the area, then you should be cautious or remove that dressing at once, and remove the plaster as well.
And take a piece of cloth and a sponge soaked in lemon juice, and dip it into boiled water, and with this wash the area until the swelling subsides and the pain is alleviated; then let the limb rest for one hour; afterwards apply on it pure wool submerged in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and bind it over it for one night until you are sure of no abscess formation, and that the swelling of the limb has subsided, and the pain from it has been relieved.
And then repeat that gentle wrapping and small plaster without tightening it as much as your first one, and gently do this until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess and hardness and pain and swelling and all the symptoms have subsided completely, and you need a plaster and wrapping, then repeat that as you did at the beginning equally.
And if no cause of those we have mentioned occurs to the limb, then do not remove it but leave it for three days or four or five or seven; and in some cases all this is left for twenty days, as seems appropriate by removing the dressing, as we said, until the fracture adheres, and the flesh coagulates over it.
At this point adjust more strictly than in your first stricture completely, and adjust according to the swelling of the patient's flesh, as was mentioned previously.
But if you see the site of the fracture already drying and wasting more than is appropriate, then you will know that nutrition is prevented from reaching it; therefore, apply a poultice to it with tepid water whenever you do so; and do this for three days in total; then slightly loosen the bandage, and through this operation direct nourishment to the member, and it will be quickly healed.
But what foolish restorers of the fractured member do otherwise when it is not restored at the proper time and in a straight manner, according to their procedure, is an error and great fear, and if this were true, the Ancients would have enlarged on this in their books and warned against it.
And I have not seen any of them do this, which is why it is more reasonable to assume that they do not do so.
Chapter Second: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct. Some fractures are caused by the impact of an object, and the impact removes part of the bone internally, just as it would in wood, and for this reason this type is called a splinter. Or the object may cut some part of the bone, cutting through the flesh but not penetrating entirely through; and this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the severity of these two fractures may be great or slight, and there is also a fracture known as comminuted or crushed; and the cause of this is an impact with something weak or falling onto a stone, and similar circumstances.
And this fracture is either penetrating that is approaching the panniculus which is below the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the danger of these two fractures is great or little.
And of the fracture that is in the bone, in the subtlety of the small fibers, and by this it is named this species of fracture capillary.
And it is the fracture that is made either by falling or by the impact of a stone and similar things, which causes some lamina of the bone to enter into its partitions, and it is made in the place of concavity as happens with the bones of the arm when they occur due to their impact, and it is the parting of their entry into the inner parts.
And this is mostly made in the bones of living beings, as with the heads of children.
And for all these kinds of fractures, treatments are used, such as pads and plasters separated. And I will return to the reminder of all this in its place.
And you shall know all these kinds of fractures by detection on them and by inquiry into their attempts and the appearance of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And the capillary fracture is known by this: it is revealed on the bone, and when it is cleaned, it appears black on the surface.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider at the beginning the symptoms of the patient, and if you see by these symptoms that it signifies by evident signs fear, such as vomiting with anger, extension, loss of understanding, hoarseness of voice, fainting, acute fever, and similar conditions along with their rigor, and if they resemble the symptoms in major dispositions, doubt whether he can be healed.
And if for uncommon cases you hope for a cure, then you begin the treatment of them.
The cause of which is that if a person comes to you with the illness at its beginning as it is an illness, and this is in the days of winter, then it is necessary for you to study the condition of the bone until the day fourteen according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you must remove the bone before the seventh day, because what is above the bone may become corrupted by inflammation, and that accidents which we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the membrane covering the brain and is in small fragments and attrition, then you must excise the fragmented and abraded part, as I will recount to you.
And you should shave the head of the patient who is ill, and uncover the bone according to custom and method as best you can according to the shape of the illness, so that it is easy for you to work on the patient.
And if it happens to you that you need to detect the bone, deal with blood flow or an abscess by cauterization, then you must proceed with what is necessary, which is to cover the area with cloths infused in vinegar and rose oil, and allow the illness to rest until the abscess is subdued and there is no more bleeding; then begin circumferential treatment around the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you excise the bone with sharp scissors, and this is the form of it: And then use another scissor slightly wider than the previous one; and this is the form of it:
And then apply some of the pus to the second incision.
And it is necessary that you have various incisions at your disposal, some of which are used for pus longer than others; and some of them are quicker in their effects than the others, and they range from sharpness to dullness, and they may be made of iron or leather.
And use them with ease in tapping on the incisions so that you do not injure the head and cause harm to it.
And if the bone is hard and strong, it is necessary to perforate around it before you apply the incisions, using shallow grooves, which are called non-deep trenches.
And they are not called this only because those do not reach the end of the skull bone to what is nearby; therefore, at the groove there is a rounded edge or margin above it near the head, similar to a small border or circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
It is convenient that you take many of these grooves, each of which suits the sharpness of each bone, so that there are present throughout the entire skull edges that are sharp, with quantities in length and shortness according to the amount of sharpness of this bone.
And this is the form of three types of grooves: The quality of perforating around the broken bone is that you place the groove on the bone and rotate it with your tools until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the groove to another location, and proceed in this manner throughout the entire area according to the quantity of its thickness or around it.
Here is the translation:
And see that you keep such careful watch that the incisor does not touch any part of the thigh.
And when you have circumscribed the perforated bone, and it is separated from the thigh if it is continuous with it, and you will extract that part, shave and equalize that in roughness to what is on the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor, but make sure it is more blunt and finer than other incisors.
And if any part of the small and delicate bones remains, now take that with ease with what is appropriate to you among the instruments; and then treat the wound with lint and dressings as we have said.
And the other method of operation is light, distant from fear, which Guy of Chauliac greatly praises; and this is the speech about it: It is necessary for you at the beginning to start revealing the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and evident, until when you have uncovered that part and you have paused below it with the tip of this slow and joyful incisor, which has this form:
And it is that the lens-shaped part should be left intact and you should not incise any part of it, while the sharp-edged part should be divided into two flaps longitudinally, as you see; so that the lens-shaped part fits against the thigh with its sharp edge fitting into the bone.
And then strike upon the incisor of one part with a small mallet until you incise all the bone with ease as it is revolved, and be sure to protect the panniculus so that no accident happens in any way that you fear, even if the practitioner were the most timid of all men and the least knowledgeable among them; certainly, and even if he were asleep.
If there is any cause associated with the bone of the neck in some places of the bone, cut that part where the lens-shaped part [of the instrument] reaches, and deliver that part from it easily, since it can be delivered without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the neck, and that which is abscised or remains of the bone is its surface, and it lies in the roughness and small particles, then you should scrape away that roughness and eradicate those particles with fine razors for which you have prepared various quantities so that you may administer the most suitable ones wherever necessary during your operation on the broken bone and use them correctly according to how you proceed.
And you should administer in your proportion at which you scrape the bone initially with the broadest of those razors, and gradually use finer ones as you proceed, and do not stop doing this continuously until you reach administering the thinnest and most delicate of all of them.
In each of the other small, delicate fractures and subtle fractures, you should administer care according to what is needed for the correction of the bone.
And it is because they are not afraid or hesitant about all those who have studied in that art. And stand on what we have written regarding major fractures.
And if the neck is swollen when you remove the bone, then take a piece of cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in warm oil, and place that over the wound opening; then take another folded or triple-layered cloth and immerse it in vinegar and warm oil, and place that over the first cloth, doing this as lightly as possible so as not to press on the cicatrix; then apply a broad bandage over it, but do not tighten it more than necessary for the wound.
If you are sure that there is no danger of an abscess after one or two days, then untie it and apply medicines which are too drying, such as lint strips and flour paste, and powder of onions and arishta, and similar things.
And it is that you should make of these medicines powder which is finely ground over the wound, as they are dry; and generally, apply any medicine whose property is to cleanse without irritating.
And be careful according to your skill that the wound in your treatment is clean; and let it not become infected or inflamed; nor allow its virulence to increase. For when it is situated over the panniculus cerebri, it corrupts that part and poisons it, and thus you will bring about a serious illness due to this malice.
And it affects the brain, as when the bone is penetrated and especially when there is pressure on the wound, and there is discoloration on its surface; and therefore consider these signs if you see the symptoms of illness that we have mentioned, for then you know it to be dangerous without doubt.
And if the discoloration is not from the wound itself but from the medicine applied above it, and it is due to the property of that medicine causing the discoloration, then take one part honey and three parts olive oil, and soak a piece of linen in these, and apply that over the brain; and thereafter treat it with appropriate healing spices until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is severed.
Know that the nose is not broken except into two main parts, or sometimes one of those lesser divisions, because in it there are two bones, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and it is not broken, but only bruised, swollen, and distorted.
If one of the two parts of it is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the side of the nose, and reduce that fracture from within with your index finger, and with your thumb without, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
And if the fracture is in the uppermost part of the nose, and your finger cannot reach it, then you should use the end of a probe that has some thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should proceed as described.
And when you restore it on the first day of the fracture if possible; and if not, then after seven or ten days, you should wait until the swelling subsides.
And afterwards insert into the end of the nose a pad of linen if the fracture is in one part, or two pads if the fracture is in two parts, and let each pad have enough thickness to fill the cavity of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the pads should be soaked in butter, and changed every day, but I have not seen this practiced; however, it is necessary that the pad be soaked in almond oil mixed with powder of flowers; and afterwards leave the pad until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
In some cases, insert in the nose, instead of cotton, a piece of wool wrapped in lion's skin; and let it be that already the fracture of the nose is so severe and that the patient not be prevented from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do that, and if you want, use cotton.
And if another swelling occurs that needs to be cauterized, apply a plaster to the nose with cerate or honey mixed with vinegar and rose oil, or with myrrh.
But if no swelling occurs that needs to be cauterized, then you should apply a plaster over it with sesame flour and powdered eggshell mixed in albumen of egg; and afterwards place it on the lion's skin, and do not cover the nose with anything.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then you should incise above them, and extract them with instruments that are suitable for this; and afterwards close the fissure, and treat it with substances that promote healing and consolidation, and with plasters appropriate for this.
And if there is an injury inside the nose, then you should treat it with cotton and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the lower jaw is broken and there is no wound on the surface, examine whether the fracture is only on the outside and not split into two parts and curved inward, then its diagnosis is easy.
If the fracture is on the right side, then you should insert your left finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left side, then at that time insert your said finger from the right hand; and push it so as to bring together the fractured bone from within with ease to the parts outside, with your other hand from the outside part of the bone; in this way properly equalize it.
And if the jaw fracture is already separated into two parts, then you should apply extension to both parts as straight as possible so that the equalization may be feasible.
And if there is concussion of the teeth with disruption, then strengthen those that you expect will break with thread of gold or silver or silk; and immediately place on the broken jaw a piece of cloth, and immediately place on it a double layer of cloth, and place on the large cloth a properly made bandage or a piece of soft material equal in length to the jaw; and then bind it above as you are accustomed to binding it and as is convenient for application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food in the form of thin soups.
And if you find that the shape has already been altered in any way, then on the third day you should examine and loosen it; and then correct what has already been altered from it and plaster it with powder of flour, egg albumen, or flaxseed meal, after you have removed the cloth from it, and place a thin layer of linen over the plaster.
And then as it stands attached and adjusted that plaster to it, and if the position of the bone is not altered, leave it so that you do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture has strengthened; for this fracture usually becomes strong in about three weeks.
And if another abscess appears which requires cauterization, then administer what we have said about similar cases during the process to ensure it is healed, and let that abscess be treated accordingly.
And if the fracture is a splinter, then consider whether the bone fragment is separated or displaced. At this point, use your skill to remove those splinters with what is necessary for their removal using the appropriate instruments.
And if the opening of the splinter is narrow, then enlarge it with a scalpel as much as your need dictates.
And when you have removed those splinters and none remain to be treated, then close the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, apply one of the plasters that are appropriate for this and promote healing until it is healed.
Chapter 5
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
There are various ways the collarbone can be fractured when broken in front of the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it may be fractured, separated, or broken into two parts without displacement; and this is easy to restore. Or it happens with displacement, making restoration difficult. Or the fracture occurs with a splinter, making it complicated.
And the procedure when the fracture occurs with a splinter is that you should have two assistants present; one to hold the displaced collarbone and assist in stabilizing it, and the other to extend the neck towards that part.
In adjusting the splints for the fracture, make sure to shape them as is appropriate, and ensure there are neither too many elevations nor depressions.
And if you need more extension, then place below the armpit of the injured side a pad made of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your necessity; extend and elevate the shoulder and compress the pad with your hand so that you equalize the fracture as is appropriate.
And if you cannot draw the end of the collarbone to the outer parts, so that it reaches its proper position, then have the patient lie supine on their neck, and place a pad over the muscle, about midway in size.
And the assistant should press the muscle from below so that the bone of the collarbone is elevated to its proper place, and then you correct the fracture and align it with the splints mentioned.
But if you feel splinters of the collarbone and small pieces, then it is necessary to cut around them and extract those splinters with ease.
And if it is stuck in the bone, then you must devise an incision with one of the scalpels that you have prepared for this purpose, after having placed below the collarbone the instrument used by the scribe; and this instrument is made of wood or iron and has the following shape:
And it is similar to the curve of a bowl, whose shape is concave; and its width should be according to your need for it, depending on the size of the bone or its smallness.
Make the length of the tool as suitable as you can for the operation; it should have two ends, and one end should be broad and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure it will form an abscess, then you should now approximate the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear it will not form an abscess, then you should employ the wound with dressings and powders according to the quantity of the wound's disruption.
And if an abscess forms, then apply a dressing soaked in vinegar and rose oil, and place it over that area.
And so that the splint is not loosened over the broken bone; and therefore it is necessary that you see the patient every day, and when the bandaging is loose or you see that the splint has already been moved, then you must correct this and tighten the bandage.
And place the patient's pillow at the back of his neck, and put something underneath to raise it so that during sleep the head is slightly elevated; thus raising the helper (scapula) on one side, and therefore elevating the fracture of the collarbone with the muscle; or have the arm bound to the side of the neck.
And do not loosen the bandaging unless you find an abscess in the vicinity, within twelve days.
And then replace the plaster if you see that it needs to be done, and adjust it in the joint and restore it so that it remains there until the fracture of the collarbone is healed and has coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the method in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some people it is done in less time.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is broken in the middle and not shattered but with its edges cracked.
And when it is broken at that location — and it is broken in the middle of it — and it is known only by palpation, then according to the figure of the fracture prepare yourself and reduce it according to its natural shape in every way you can manage.
And then put on the place powder of ground egg whites and lion's fat; and then put on powder of double cloth; and then put on the plate lint very finely according to the quantity of the spoon all or at most a little.
And if you leave the plate concave in some parts of the spoon, then you fill that concavity with lion's fat, and then press the plate according to equality.
And then bind it above with linen strips with moderate tension and fix the plate in place so that it does not move from its position.
And examine the bandaging each day; and every time it is softened, apply another layer and tighten it, and adjust the plate if it has moved from its position.
And place the dressing on its proper side.
And the spoon is restored in xx days or in xx.v days.
And when this number of days is completed, then you remove the bandaging, and be sure that these are bones for which there was no initial fear nor dissolution.
And if pus appears in the bone area and you place a bandage there, then you cut into it, remove it, and proceed as we have said for the fracture of the clavicle or for the suppuration of an abscess, should that occur here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of foot fractures.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle and remains relatively simple; however, its extremities often move, causing cicatricization and comminution.
Of the accidents when the bone is broken are that it inclines towards the inner parts and causes great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, and spitting blood; and it is manifest in the cavity of the broken bone and not hidden from feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on their back, and a bolster is placed between the shoulders; then press the muscles, and adjust the ribs with two or more ribs, and delicately perform the operation and adjustment in every possible way suitable for this purpose, until the figure of the bone returns as necessary.
And then place the plaster and cotton over thin laths of wood or reeds or similar light materials, as you wrap it in cloth; then carefully bind that over the broken bone without pressing too hard; and go around the binding according to the roundness of the back several times, and tighten that with moderate strength; then observe the bandage at all times, and when it softens, tighten it again.
And if necessity requires the removal of that due to corrosion or swelling, or pain, or abscess which occurs in the area, then you must cut and loosen that and remove the plaster and rectify what has occurred according to the method of treatment; and then reapply the plaster, if it is necessary for this purpose; and adjust the binding until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in thick parts where they lie next to each other; and at the extremities of them in front, such contusions do not happen there because they are cartilaginous. And concerning the nerve, there is no deception regarding its sensation; you must investigate it as mentioned.
And the restoration should be such that it equalizes the fracture as mentioned by the possible method, within which it is aligned as necessary, and then apply a plaster and strengthen the broken bone with a splint if you deem it necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs inclines towards the inner parts, it often causes severe pain and piercing sensations, like those that occur when one weeps, and because of this the bone puts on a covering; and there is difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the Ancients were skilled in that which involves generating terms.
And what they say is: it is necessary to avoid foods that cause inflammation and gas, so that the abdomen does not become swollen and extended, and thus the fracture is pushed towards the outer parts.
But we should be cautious with this to avoid it being a light cause for generating an abscess, if it does not occur; and if it does occur, then it helps in dealing with that.
And some of them say that it should be paused above the application of gas relief and then suck out with strength; this is similar to reasoning, but it is feared that the gas might draw the excesses to the site due to weakness.
And some of them say: it is necessary to cover the area with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and place poultices between the ribs so that it is filled, making the bandaging even as you wrap it according to its roundness; then treat the injury as one would for pleurisy with food therapy and medicine.
And if the patient is very constrained by pain which they cannot endure, and the bone has penetrated through the dressing with a puncture wound, we fear for the patient; at that time it is necessary to cut above the location, expose the broken part, and immediately apply an instrument to secure the fracture, the memory of which precedes, and that we easily cut the bone and extract it.
And immediately adjust the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs internally, then carefully incise it and apply powder in boiled oil on the site, and treat the patient with what is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let them be reclined on the side on which they sleep until they are healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine of the back and neck.
In some cases, fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and similarly in their healing, as well as in the spine of the back. And when such an accident happens to someone and you want to know if they will recover or not, then consider this: if the veins are already softened, numb, and dead, and cannot be moved or stretched, and when you press on them with your fingers or pierce them with a needle and there is no pain in them, then you know that they will not recover, and in such conditions it is mortal.
And if they respond and there is constriction when you press on them and pierce them with a needle, then you know that the bone in the neck is safe and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck like this and want to know whether it will heal or not, then look at its condition.
Now if you see that someone has become weak, and it happens in the way that usually happens to most people, and shortly after lying on their back they feel windiness and involuntary evacuation, and sometimes they are bent backward and want to urinate but cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and at that point do not strive for a cure for them.
But if none of those causes occur, then the condition is better and farther removed from danger.
And the treatment for them is to apply a poultice to reduce the abscess after placing over the affected vertebra olive oil that has been slightly heated, or two eggs that are boiled; place this on it several times during the day until the abscess is reduced.
And now apply one of the soothing and restorative plasters, and wrap it with a bandage; and instruct the patient to use rest and calm, and not to sleep on the side where they feel pain until it is healed.
And if it happens that the bone is disjointed or any cause that has separated it, then you should reduce it and set the bone; and afterwards approximate the two lips of the wound, stitching if it is large; and then treat it with some flesh-forming ointment until it is healed.
But if the end of the bone is broken at the joint and it is the tip of the bone, then you should insert your finger into the buttock with the left hand, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by second intention; and then apply a plaster and splint to it, if needed, and wrap it.
And if you find wounds with broken bones in them, then immediately attend to that area, remove what is necessary, and treat the injury with the method described previously until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the broad area (likely referring to the abdomen or a specific anatomical region).
The bones of the broad area when they are broken do not simply communicate at their ends but are split along their length and deviate to the sides; and pain and swelling occur in the location where the fracture has occurred, and movement is impaired when there is a fracture.
And the restoration of it is that you place your hand upon it so that it extends over the fracture until it assumes the shape it should have.
And if the fracture is at the end of it only, then you must immobilize that part as seems appropriate to you by means of equal binding until it assumes its natural shape.
But if the fracture is along the length or has deviated to the sides, then you make the injured person lie on their back until you are prepared for the restoration of that fracture. And when you have immobilized that part, apply a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or cane next to it, and bind it tightly with bandages so that there is no risk of fracture movement or displacement.
And fill the cavity with something that will keep it in place until the bandaging is even and symmetrical, and instruct the injured person to lie on their back or side.
If an inflamed abscess is cauterized, then immediately you should cease the extension of it and its restoration until the cauterized abscess is calmed, and pause on what precedes that; and subsequently return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it as is appropriate.
And if you encounter bones that are injured or broken, then you should treat them.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist.
The adjutori is what is between the forearm and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is fractured, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a flat splint of moderate thickness in this form:
And let them be attached at both ends with two bandages, and then it should be suspended from an elevated location.
And let the patient lie on a mattress; and then place the injured arm, which is fractured, onto the splint until it reaches the midpoint where the curvature of the splint is attached.
And then place some heavy object on it, or let the patient extend that arm downward; and then the physician should secure the fracture with his hands, so that the fracture returns to its proper position.
And the other method is that the patient lies supine on their neck and suspend the arm at the neck with bandages; and then instruct two assistants, one of whom holds what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other holds what is below it; and each of them pulls in their respective directions.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then wrap one bandage around the fracture and another bandage over that, and each of the two assistants should pull on their respective ends.
And if the fracture is close to the muscle's extremity, then wrap half of the bandage over the pad and the other half below the fracture, pulling it tight.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the joint, then wrap the bandage over that area and around that joint.
And do the same with ease and care. As soon as it is properly adjusted and applied correctly, then tighten that bandage if no swelling occurs.
But if swelling occurs, then loosen the bandage within seven days, and wrap it with wool soaked in vinegar and rose water until the swelling subsides, and then tighten that bandage.
And the method of tightening that bandage is to apply a plaster over the fracture, and then wrap a cloth strip around the plaster; and then adjust the arm using that support, and place your hand on their muscle.
And wrap the cloth and tighten around the support so that the arm is in a fixed position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits it.
And now use splints.
And place on the fracture that splint which is the strongest and largest of the other splints; and place between each splint a width equal to the breadth of a finger, and let the length of the splints follow the break with an addition of three fingers on all sides.
And then place a bandage over the stitches which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage over the site of the fracture be even tighter. And the farther apart the fracture is, the bandage should be looser.
And therefore, when you place the splints and apply the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then do so.
And if you fear an abscess may form, then leave the bandage and splints in place for seven days as we have said.
And then examine the limb in all parts for three days so that no swelling or inflammation occurs at the site; or it is prohibited for food to reach the limb due to excessive bandaging; and therefore, correct all of this as we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause of this will occur, then do not loosen the bandage for three days or longer.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head should be above their stomach.
And apply a pad filled with evenly distributed wool over the splint, and at all times of the day or night ensure that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage has become loose; therefore, correct this entirely with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned earlier, such that the food is light at first until bandaging begins; and then gradually make the food heavier.
It is customary for the stitches to remain in place for forty days; and at this time they should be removed, a bath should be given, and medicine should be applied with remedies that correct it.
And if the fracture is comminuted, then you should not remove the bandage and stitches for one day or even up to two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the ulna and radius; one of which is small and is that which lies towards the thumb, and the other is larger and is that which is placed below the smaller one, towards the outer side.
And if the fracture is of the larger bone only, or of the smaller one, or both together.
And when the smaller bone is fractured above, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the bone is fractured below, the fracture is severe, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are fractured together.
Therefore, if the bone that is fractured is the smaller one above, then it is appropriate for the physician, during its restoration, to allow a slight extension with ease until it is set.
But if the larger bone is the one that is fractured, then it is necessary to allow more vigorous extension.
And if both bones are broken, now it is necessary for you to make the incision more strongly.
And it is necessary to place the hand in a position for restoration and extension stretched out on a cushion, with the wrist above all parts, so that all other fingers are properly aligned, and the thumb be lower than the others, and the patient lie seated on a chair.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at the height or straight ahead, so that the patient does not have to strain.
And let the assistant extend the arm from below with the patient's hand or a bandage, and another assistant extend it similarly from above.
And then the doctor should align the bone so that it is reduced into its best position as possible.
And if you encounter difficulty in setting the bone, now you must reduce all parts to their proper place with your skill.
And if there appears a separate difficulty that impedes the joint and is not within your hope for restoration, now you should act on it and remove that according to the method we have mentioned above.
But if the fracture is due to inflammation, I have already made a separate chapter on this, so follow the treatment described there.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, now you should line a cloth with cerot made of rose oil and white wax, and it should be midway between thickness and fineness, and place a finely strained cloth over it so that the abscess is reduced; then remove the cerot and apply a plaster prepared from powdered herbs mixed with egg whites, and afterwards place bandages; and let the bandage placed over that fracture be wider and firmer.
And know that the number of stars on the arm can be seen in major dispositions, or whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones simultaneously.
And then pause your traction on the site of the fracture as firmly and vigorously, and when you want to perform the traction above or below the fracture, pause the traction a little more gently according to how it was preceded in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that you wrap around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is strengthened should be of medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the bandaging after some days. And if any cause occurs for which correction is necessary, such as inflammation that occurs in the limb, then you should immerse the limb in hot water until the inflammation subsides.
And let the limb not be constrained until it rests, and then resume the traction.
And if the traction has already been relaxed and the bone is already displaced as it seems, then you should correct all of this with your skill.
And consider whether the nutrition is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive traction, for then you should relax it slightly and let it rest for some days until the nutrition reaches it, and then strengthen it.
And if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to release them for about 20 days or thereabouts.
And so the hand of the patient is suspended by being tied to something above, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in xxx days or in xxxii days, or at the lightest it is restored in xxviii days, and all of this depends on the disposition of the tissues and the strength thereof.
Chapter XIII.
On the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and of the said bones.
Know that to the thumb of the hand and to the joints of the said bones, when they suffer fracture, no other injury occurs except slight damage.
And when a fracture or injury occurs to the hand, it is necessary that the patient remain lying flat and facing upwards according to equality, and place the hand extended upon that.
And then the attendant should extend the broken bones, and the physician should adjust them so that they are properly set.
And now it is necessary to apply a plaster and cotton, unless an abscess has occurred, in which case cauterization may be needed; then place a splint above according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that with cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, then make it appear as a sphere with cloth, and instruct the patient to hold that with their fractured palm; then bind it tightly with cloth.
And let the splint be of wood wrapped in cloth in which it is raised, so that the wood is applied to the palm; and bind that as needed.
If the fracture is in the bones of the forearm, you should place a splint above and another below the affected part, so that the hand may be open and straight; and immediately apply bandages, as if wrapping the hand around a bowl with the mentioned bandages.
And if a fracture occurs in one of the bones mentioned, you should adjust the finger as necessary and immediately straighten it on the palm; and if you wish, place a small splint to raise the fracture and prevent it from being compressed.
But if the fracture is in any of the others mentioned, such as in the middle bone, or in the index finger, or in the ring finger, you should adjust and bandage it to the corresponding finger; or bind them all together in continuity, which is better; or place a small splint on that one, as we have said for the fingers.
And consider, in your restoration and immediately after that, the disposition of abscesses and opposing treatments — during the operation — according to what is necessary when any cause of those conditions arises, as it is repeatedly described in the text.
Chapter XIV.
On the restoration of a fracture of the collarbone.
The collarbone, which is often broken, is revealed by sensation according to how it has shifted in the affected area and the most prominent part.
And the restoration is done in such a way that one bandage is tightly wrapped above the fracture and another below it, and the patient lies prone on their stomach and face.
And so the attendant should extend the bandage according to its quality when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
And if it extends towards the root of the spine, then the bandage should be applied to the back at the part above the fracture, so that the extension falls on it, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture extends towards the back, then the bandage should be applied to the back, and the extension should be to the parts below.
And so the physician should equalize the bone on both sides but within such that it is reduced according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by good union; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and strapping, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess does occur, then you should leave it alone for several days until the abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The strapping should be done as follows: you should wrap around the fracture a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap the limb so that it reaches to the ankle and the root of the spine.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the spine and the limb near the back at the lower parts, and let the ends of the cloth extend from the most prominent part of both sides, and then wrap around the limb and the spine any excess bandage.
And then place strips of cloth over the spine at the location of the fracture, and place one strip directly over the bone of the limb.
And fill the empty space between the bowl and the container with soft cloths, so that the constriction may be coagulated.
And then begin the firm bandaging from above on the place of the fracture with three or four layers, and as much as you distance yourself with the binding from the place of the fracture, your bandaging should be smaller, lighter, and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the bandage to the sides below until it reaches the nail of the foot, and tighten both ends of the bandages from the other side so that the binding does not be moved from its place.
And then place the bandage in such a way that corrosion, pus, swelling, or similar conditions do not occur on the limb; and if any cause of these should arise, cut it off immediately and correct what has happened as soon as possible, as we have said before.
But if there is a piece of bone that needs to be placed in the bone where it belongs, then you should try to fit it there as best as possible; and if not, fix it and move it, and treat it according to what precedes until it is healed.
And in some cases, this fracture of the bone is tightened with another bandage, as we have said regarding the helper and the arm.
But during the restoration of the limb in such a manner done, no lameness should occur to the patient; and if it is restored alone or with another bandage applied, then it is necessary that the person will limp permanently.
And know that the swelling lasts for forty days or a few months, and all of this depends on the diversity of constitutions and other conditions.
Chapter XV.
On the restoration of the fracture of the lower jawbone.
Know that the fracture of the lower jawbone usually occurs in this manner, but injuries also happen from blows coming from above.
But if the fracture happens, it is often merely a crack or contusion in its parts, and this is due to one or several injuries, and all of this affects the sensation.
And the restoration of it is such that what is broken in its parts must be equalized, and what is separated must be brought together and joined as much as possible by adjustment, ease, and construction.
And then apply a plaster and place it over that round bandage if you need one; and wrap it tightly above the fracture as is appropriate.
And then consider all the precautions we have recommended for other fractures, just as with abscesses and similar conditions, to prevent any accidents that might oppose healing until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the chin.
Know that in the chin there are two bones, one of which is thick and is called the chin bone; the other is slender and is called the mental bone.
And it happens with some of the types of fracture that happen to both bones of the arm, and by this restoration is made just as the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation of it is one.
And if you break both bones together, the curvature is distributed to all parts.
And if the more delicate bone is broken, the curvature is transferred to the forward parts.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it below, and it is from those causes that you are not at fault.
And promptly administer extension and alignment and fixation of the fragments according to that manner.
But it is necessary, if the fracture of the curvature is jagged with multiple fragments, that the extension be gentle and slower, and exercise caution with your approach.
And when there is any cause for operation on the arm regarding the curvature, and when you align the fragments and complete the entire procedure, at that time you will take pieces of wood from a tree that grows below the causes which are set in water, and made similarly from palm branches; choose those with slight roughness, and not too thick, and their length according to the length of the curvature, and place them so that they are closest to the bottom.
And then wrap around each one a cloth in two layers according to the length of it, and place one at the side of the curvature on one side and the other on the opposite side, and they should be from the bottom to the lowest part of the body.
Place the two struts in three places at both extremities and at the middle place, for with this structure the arch is prohibited from tilting to the right and left, and it stands in good position.
And now a brace of wood has been administered according to the length of the arch; and let it be placed in such a way that the arch can maintain its mobility.
And especially do this in the case of a fracture, and when it is properly a case of such an injury.
And every day consider the arch and study it carefully based on previous studies, and guard it from abscesses, inflammation, or other similar causes; and when any of these causes occur, then you must act accordingly until it is healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the talus and of the bones of the foot and of the ones mentioned.
A complete fracture does not usually occur in the talus; but fractures can happen occasionally to the bones of the foot; and to those bones of the foot, few suffer fractures, and instead they mostly experience strains due to their disposition.
And if the bones of the foot sustain a fracture and the bones are raised above their normal position, then the injured person should place their foot on prepared ground as described.
And then place the foot on what is elevated by those bones, and then step on them, press, and bandage them until they return to their proper places.
And then apply a plaster and bandage above it, and below the arch of the foot place a small board with two padded ends.
From around the wound take the flesh which is attached to the descending structure, since you have wrapped that area in cloths and other materials for which you need.
And when three or four days have passed, you then untie the bandage, for you will find the bones either united, or broken or dislocated, and the operation on those cases is as you know.
But if anyone says they are broken, then you should reset them and unite them as I recommend to you in the resetting of the bones of the hand. And then place the broken finger on a splint according to the length of the finger, and leave some space beyond it; and afterwards attach this board, which I recommend to you, to the descending structure.
But if what is broken are two or three or more bones, then you place a splint of appropriate length wrapped in linen over each finger, and attach the board at the base of the foot, and let it rest on one end of the board so that it provides good support for the foot.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes mentioned to you in other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and prepare yourself for every eventuality with this knowledge.
Chapter XVIII.
Of the fracture of the pubic bone in women and of the bone of the penis and the shaft of the penis in men.
When the pubic bone of a woman is broken, then perform the surgery in a reclined position, and afterwards incline her slightly to one side, and have her held securely behind.
Let the midwife fill the basin with cotton wool, and let it be done in the basin as described; then let the woman be placed on her back, and raise the buttocks little by little, for all of this is to ensure that everything is expelled from within, including the remains after delivery as described and the broken bone returns.
Then place poultices on his back, and when he wants to urinate, raise the cotton to facilitate it, and after urination reduce them and return them according to the method by which they were mixed at the beginning, and then return to his first poultice.
And do this for seven days or around that time, for he will be restored then.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and tie one end of a reed to it; then insert the entire bladder into his body through an opening, and blow air into the reed until the bladder is inflated inside the body, for this will help the fracture to heal.
And then fill it with cotton and keep it for several days as we have recommended, until he is healed.
And when the bone of the hip joint in a man or woman is fractured, proceed with its restoration and alignment as we have recommended for the fracture of the hip.
And what is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures, and few discover it; mostly for the one who has studied and used this book of mine well; for that fracture signifies to you in the major positions the correct way in restoration and in constraining it, and therefore you understand well.
Take linen bandages when the penis is grasped, and put the penis in them; then press upon its wrapping of cloth, and it should be wrapped up, and thereafter left for three days or about that time, and it will heal.
Chapter XIX.
On the restoration of fractured bones when there is swelling.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with swelling, especially if the bone is large like the femur or the humerus, or similar to them, that you cauterize and incise it immediately if the conditions for cauterization and incision indicate this, as we have said above.
And if the swelling discharges blood, then it is appropriate to apply something to arrest this, such as powdering over it powdered turpentine, unless some other cause prevents you from doing so.
And then begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess forms, then you should postpone the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day, for doing so would cause harmful complications.
But if the broken bone protrudes above the skin, then it is appropriate to reduce and equalize it with your fingers as easily and with minimal extension as possible.
But if extension is not prepared and equalization with your fingers is not possible, then you should reduce it using this instrument; the instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight digits, and its width according to the size of the swelling. It is necessary for the surgeon to have three or four such instruments in different sizes for use in all types of fractures.
And let it be round, and let there be little grooves in it so that it is not doubled up; press it upon him during the operation.
And let it have a sharp point at which there is a margin in its extremities; and let the upper part be thick tending, and from the middle to the parts divided.
And this is the form of it:
And it is called in Greek "labrum voluntas atelarem," which means a small palette.
So that the extremity of it may be placed on the elevated part of the bone.
And press it in one place where the bone should return, and let it be balanced with some equation.
And thus strive to balance the ends of the fracture between them, and if the end of the broken bone is slender, and does not take that tool as a good instrument, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone so that the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely as we have recommended, then incise it with that which is suitable among the knives of which we have spoken; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever manner is possible for you.
And smooth that which remains on the bone of roughness and small scratches.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient feels intense pain in the tooth, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and then do what will be of great benefit to the patient.
When you have completed the restoration that you perform on the bone, and now you should dip a cloth in black styptic wine, and properly if it is summer, and do not apply cerot or any other cause which may contain oil over the wound, so that corruption and putrefaction does not occur there.
And immediately after you administer plates at the time of your completion of the restoration of the bone, and leave the wound uncovered so that you can excise the bruised tissue with scissors and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And be careful not to leave the wound on the fracture, for many times unskilled doctors have done this, and it has caused death or a corrosive illness or sepsis in their patients. And if the swelling is slight, soft, and different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or it is a bad wound, and you fear some of the harmful accidents we have mentioned, and you feel pain in that area, then do not use plates over that wound, but make a bandage of hard cloths instead of plates and apply it to the wound.
And after one day or two, the wound will begin to form granulation tissue, at which point you remove the cloth that you had placed over it with wine, and then administer medicines, such as those we commonly use to treat wounds, including tetramericon and similar remedies.
And it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage and examine the wound every morning and evening until it is healed and consolidated, and it should be prepared in such a way that putrefaction can run off from it to the lower parts with ease.
If after several days the swelling has not subsided and it is not separated from the bone, then you should know that here you are dealing with small ones because you inquire about the swelling in this way. And of those small swellings, those that can be separated and removed and extracted, those that cannot be separated and are left in the limb causing pain, pierce them as you incise them according to what is possible for you.
And if the swelling becomes inflamed or pus-filled, which is a sign of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you pause to choose one of those methods according to what is suitable for the treatment, as preceded in its narrative chapter.
And regarding the matter in which my speech should be focused and you present your effort: when a bone is fractured, it protrudes from the limb as does the bone of the wing and the helper bones and similar to those of larger limbs, and do not presume traction on it, nor its extraction, for after many attempts this may lead to death; leave that inside so it can heal, for it will likely fall out naturally within about 20 or 30 days; and then treat the swelling if visible, otherwise leave it as is.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of knots that occur in the aftermath of some fracture.
This knot often occurs in the aftermath of the healing of a fracture, especially near the joints, and it disfigures the shape of the limb; and frequently prevents the limb from performing its natural functions.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer medicines that are astringent such as aloes, myrrh, mira, sercacollo, acrassia, and similar ones; take some of those or all of them, and confess them with either stiptic wine or white hawthorn wine or sweet wine, and apply this over the swelling in lint, and compress it tightly over it.
And let the bandaging be such that you do not loosen it for several days; and then loosen it and replace it with another one until the swelling is reduced; or apply a sheet of lead gently over it, because lead has the property of reducing all that is swollen in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned into a hardened mass and is causing severe pain requiring immediate removal, then cut it at its highest point, and excise the excess or reduce it with some remedies until it is reduced; and continue treating it until it is healed.
Chapter XXI.
On the restoration of a fracture when it is set and subsequently the limb is moved gently but according to its natural course.
When a bone fracture is set and subsequently the limb is moved gently but improperly, this is done for various reasons: one of which is too much loosening of the bandages and ligatures not as required; or excessive compression of the bandages preventing proper nutrition from reaching the limb; or excessive use of poultices; or improper movement outside its appropriate time; or lack of sufficient blood in the patient's body and weakness.
For treatment, give the patient food to eat so that the body becomes soft inside where blood may multiply therein; and administer baths; and apply poultices and linen bandages upon it.
And apply plasters to the limb, so that the plaster attracts nourishment; or continue rubbing with water-based unguents until nutrition flows and returns it to its natural shape.
Chapter XXII.
On the treatment of broken bones when they have healed improperly and prohibit their operation in such a way as is necessary.
When it happens to a limb that has already been restored near the healing of its curvature and prominence in the broken bone and knotting, and this is due to the deformation of the shape of the limb, but the limb is not prohibited from its natural operation, then there is no need for you to heed the words of one who believes that member to be newly broken.
But such stand the words of foolish physicians and restorers who have done this in our land; and this operation is a reprehensible practice leading to great fear, the sign of which is fatal.
But if limping and knotting are recent, then it is necessary to rub them with water-based unguents in which soothing herbs are mixed, such as mallows, linseed, and melilot, and similar ones; and a plaster should be applied that has a softening effect, well prepared as such; or take mucilage of linseed and mix it with flour of chicken or olive oil, then apply it as a plaster; or take fatty figs and grind them with dove fat, and similarly use medicines that are mentioned for strengthening.
And in some cases it is dissolved by constant gentle friction which is applied to the most fluid parts, and movement of the member should be administered to all parts at all times.
If tortuosity has already occurred and it is made strong and converted into bone and there is a need for treatment with iron, then pus must be evacuated and the continuation of the bone must be dissolved, and what is superfluous from the nodes of that tissue near the bone should be cut with fine instruments, and ease should be provided in this regard with diligence and study.
Chapter XXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints from its proper place, thereby prohibiting movement, and it distorts the figure of the member, causing severe pain and great injury.
When a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to immediately attempt its reduction and not delay in doing so, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and strong adhesions form, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to move or stand during the period of inflammation, as movement often causes spasm and severe pain.
But when this occurs, it is necessary to prepare for the incision of the affected area, and then allow rest until the inflammation subsides; afterward, the member should be bathed in hot water and oil, and then reduction should be attempted with ease.
And the entire member should be treated with what will help its memory to return to its proper place.
Here are the bones set in order according to what was mentioned before about fractures; now I enter into the part concerning lesser wounds.
Chapter XXIIIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
When both jaws are dislocated and not locked; the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are moved from their positions slightly and then relaxed a little, or they are completely dislocated and fully separated until they become relaxed at the joint where saliva flows, and the person cannot hold anything nor close their mouth, and the tongue is wrapped within the cheek.
But when the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its proper position by itself with little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and thorough, then prompt reduction must be administered and should not be delayed.
And it is that the minister hold the patient's head, and the physician insert one thumb into the root of the dislocated jaw inside the mouth if the dislocation is on one side; or if the dislocation is on both sides, he should place his thumbs together in both roots, while the other fingers remain outside symmetrically; and he instructs the patient to relax their jaws and allow them to move apart from each side; and the physician gently guides the jaw back into place until it returns to its proper location.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer warm water and oil for evaporation, until gentle reduction can be performed on both sides and do not delay the reduction of both entirely, as we have said.
And so when returning to these bandages, and the mouth of the wound is closed but not softened, then sprinkle over those powders from linen dipped in wax, and it should be made of wax and fine oil; and afterwards it should be applied with ease using soft cloths.
And let the patient sleep on their side, and their head be positioned between their two ears, so that it does not turn to either the right or left; and do not force them to chew for any reason; have their food be cut up fine until such time as when the pains are relieved and the jaws are loosened, then let them eat what they wish.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not work on the opening of the mouth for eating and drinking and speech until such time as the jaw is loosened and healed.
And if the reduction of both jaws when they are dislocated at some time and not returned to their proper places is difficult, then fevers and constant sweats often occur, and the patient's belly is easily distended, and vomiting pure bile frequently happens.
And when you see this, then you know that it is hopeless; and often the one to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter XXV.
On the Reduction of the Dislocation of the Clavicle and the Extremity of the Muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the chest inward towards the hands; and sometimes it is dislocated outward, and this is evident by sensation.
For the restoration of the hand, the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. And then press the area with your palm firmly, for it will return.
And then apply the poultice and powder to it.
But the extremity of the collarbone that is connected to it is not dislocated on any day; therefore, it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and according to your preparation.
And then apply the poultice, powder, and bandage, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And in this method of treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is when it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is when it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this happens slowly; and it does not dislocate at the rear parts by the shoulder; and it does not dislocate at the front parts by the nerves.
And it is most commonly dislocated outward at the lower parts below the joint, and more frequently in those whose flesh is thin, it easily becomes dislocated; but in those with thick muscles, it remains dislocated with difficulty.
If it happens that someone is struck or falls and a muscle becomes swollen with inflammation and is cauterized because it has already been dislocated.
Then you should proceed with what is necessary for treating it, and now you can begin the treatment of it.
