And the dislocation of it is known when it is in the lower parts near the tendons; therefore, you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal one, for you notice a clear difference among them; and you find at the head of the muscle a concavity and below the tendon the touch of the head of the muscle as if by a man's hand, and the injured person cannot return his hand to its proper place, nor can he move it at all.
If it is dislocated from the feet or above, then you immediately notice a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And this dislocation is easily reduced when it is fresh and the injured person is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the practitioner lifts the hand of the injured person to the upper parts; then places his fingers below the tendon and raises the joint firmly back into its place, and the practitioner lifts the hand of the injured person and extends it out straight; then waits until it returns easily.
But if it does not return as we have said, even after some days from the beginning of the dislocation, then the injured person should be bathed in warm water and a poultice administered to soften and relax it as if for old contusions, using raisin stones, fenugreek, and melilot in water, which should be applied.
Let the patient lie on their back with their buttocks just below the tip of the leather cylinder that is balanced between levity and strictness, and then the surgeon places his hand on the sphere, raises the muscle upwards, and pulls the patient's arm so that it is not harmed by the parts behind, and another assistant should hold the patient's head so that it is not injured by the parts behind, for it immediately returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce that second method in this manner: that is to have a man present who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on the side of that flank, and insert his hand below the tumor of the patient and raise the tumor towards above so that the patient hangs in the air, and another assistant pulls the patient's arm from behind the patient’s stomach. But if the patient is lighter, it is necessary to have something else that can be used for suspending him, so that he may be weighed down by it, because the dislocation immediately returns.
And it is similarly reduced to another method: that is to fix in the ground a long pole with its top being round like a mortar head, neither thick nor thin, and then place it below the patient; once you have placed over the top of the pole strong cloths, let the patient stand along the length of the pole, and then extend his arm towards behind on one side; and extend his body from the other side with strength, for the joint immediately returns to its place.
And if the reduction of it is difficult due to all the causes we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a pole whose length is two cubits, and its width be four fingers, and the thickness of it should be tapered at the tip according to this form:
And above the round head place linen cloths so that the skull does not come into contact with the diseased area.
And let it be paused below the armpit of the patient, and let the arm or hand be extended over the ribcage towards the lower parts, and let the body be wrapped around the helper's arms and hands.
Let the arm be placed on a ladder step according to the latitude, and let the hand be extended towards the lower parts, and let the other side of the body be suspended from the other side, for the joint will soon follow.
And when the instrument is completed as it is possible for you to do, now place below the armpit a pad of sheep's wool of equal thickness; then place an adhesive prepared from powdered moly and egg white over all the muscle, as mentioned above, and afterwards tightly bandage below the armpit with proper tension. The bandaging should be wound around the plaster, and the hand should be attached to the neck, and left alone, without moving the hand for seven days.
And it is necessary to give the patient little food until the limb is strengthened, for it is easier for them to recover in this way.
And then food should be given more freely after seven or five days, and at this point one movement can be exercised; and thus when it is firm and not softened, now it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated due to swelling that occurs or for some other reason, then it should be treated with cautery applied in three tracts, as was said earlier in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done and after about seven days the bandage is removed and the joint has not healed, and the plaster and strapping are reapplied several times, and it does not heal and cannot be raised to a higher position, then you know that the nerves of it, which run at the head of the muscle, have either been cut or stretched or softened, and understand that the joint will never again be fixed in its place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs from the feet and the mamilla or the parts behind them, the reduction is performed with impulse and extension as needed, until it returns to its place.
And let the other strapping be applied, and care for it until it is healed.
And if heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement occur near the time of healing, then the patient should use baths several times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter XXVII.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Neck.
Know that the joint of the neck is dislocated with difficulty and seems to return with difficulty; and it can be dislocated in all directions, but mostly forward or backward. The dislocation of it is not concealed from you as when lying down after a fall or according to some figure it has been dislocated, and when you adjust the dislocated neck, this is manifestly shown to you because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm nor touch its own muscle.
And you should treat the neck, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes inflamed, for if it becomes inflamed, reduction is difficult, and even if healed, it will not be fully restored; especially when it is in the rear parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocations, more painful, and often leads to death.
And the restoration of it, when it is such that it can be reduced, is that the hand of it should be extended at two fingers, and the arm of it should be expanded, and the fingers of the physician should be above the joint and below it, and he presses the joint with his fingertips together, or with the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
If the dislocation is to the front parts, then it returns thus that he bends the hand with his hand, until he strikes the joint with the palm of his hand.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong movement.
And it is that two ministers extend the arm, and hold it in such a way that it is not retracted due to the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated from all sides, since it is wrapped in folded cloth long over the hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he anoints it with some oil so that this helps in the lubricity of the joint for ease; and then he presses the joint vigorously until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary that he rest on a splint in which there is firmness and stability with linen bandages, and be restrained with moderate compression, and the arm be suspended from the neck, and be left for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and thus if the joint is formed in its proper place, now you undo the bandage of it and leave it.
And if you see that the joint should not be stitched, then now apply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for some days until it is healed; and afterward untie that.
And if it happens that there is severe pain near the reduction of the joint, and its position is in movement, then administer hydration by bath and gentle friction, and compression, until it is calmed; or place on the joint something oily, which is an agent of smooth motion; and afterward bind it, and leave it on the bone day and night. And afterward remove that, and place it in a bath; and when dry, then you should rub the joint with gentle friction, and afterward apply something oily over it, which is an agent of smooth motion, once, and the second time, and the third time, in the bath instrument, until it is finished.
And if you wish, place on that humid and heated cow's hide over it; and do this for several times, since that is moist and helps to return it to its proper position.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow joint, which is the joint between the arm and the hand.
The dislocation of the hand sometimes occurs, and the reduction of such a dislocation is easy, but the opposite is true for other joints. Nevertheless, it is necessary to act with speed in reducing it at the hour when it is dislocated, before swelling or inflammation sets in.

However, it must be considered whether the palm is already placed on the abdomen of the hand. If it is diseased, place the back of the patient's hand on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation precedes from the back of the hand, then the position of the hand should be directly on the table so that the doctor’s hand can act upon that proximity of the joint.
And if it returns quickly, that is good; but if not, then strengthen that area with a bandage over the abscess and leave it be; and do not repeat this action, for otherwise, it will neither endure nor allow you to reduce the pus that passes each day.
However, the joint moves according to its tortuousness; and if you notice in the patient no cause for this, except that the hand is weakened and cannot strengthen any cause; then you will know that the nerves are injured or severed, and therefore it should not be treated by strengthening but rather with cauterization near the location of the injury and fortifying what remains without attributing it to a specific cause.

Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the said joints.
In some cases, the said joints are dislocated completely.
When a finger is dislocated either towards the thumb or towards the palm, straightaway hold the finger and gently push it back into place.
And then hold the tip of the finger, and suspend it above the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like this for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is properly bandaged on that day.
And when it is night, wrap it as described, and do not stop unwrapping it during the day and exercising its movement, and wrap it at night; and do this for several days until it is strong.
And do the same for this if it is dislocated toward the palm of the hand, and wrap it in that part as you would for the first operation until it is healed.
And do the same when it is dislocated to other parts.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the spinal vertebrae.
When a complete dislocation occurs to any of the spinal vertebrae in the back or neck and they move out of their proper places, then there is no cure for this, as death is feared for the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's excretion occurs without their will and they cannot control it; often, certain parts of the body become numb, such as one or both sides of the body or one or both arms; or a vertebra has moved from its place, as sometimes it moves and sometimes there is a displacement in four directions.
Thus it is exposed at the posterior parts and is named gelbozitat.
The treatment for this is to consider whether the gelbozitation occurs due to pus, because in that case there is no cure and there is no remedy for it.
But in the case where it comes from trauma or impact or something similar to these, the Ancients described the methods of treatment at length, which I will summarize for brevity; but little excuses me from excess due to my closeness to understanding and explanation, with which I have explained and formulated this out of instruments different from those with which I would show this and explain that.
And those are not cured except for the one that occurs precisely as I recount.
And it is that the patient lie on their back near a wall, extended below a padded support so that the feet are not pressed.
And place a straight pole, planted in the ground at the end near the head, and another pole around the feet at the other end of the bed.
And the attendant holds one pole, and it is not fixed in a pit; and another attendant holds the other according to that form.
And then wrap a bandage around the head of the patient and under the armpits tightly; extend the end of the bandage to the pole that is behind the head, and secure it there; and then stretch another bandage over the hips near the buttocks; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole that is behind the feet.
And so the minister shall extend the bandages at the part of the stump, and let not the two stumps and their respective bandages be moved from the places in which they are fixed, but rather let them not be fastened as we have said.
And the physician shall place his palms on the strong part of the spine until it returns, or he should place upon that one table and then adjust upon the table his own weight until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, now take a table whose width or length is about three arms' lengths, and dig into the wall near the patient a place where the end of the table can be inserted. And then place the middle of the table on the spine, and the physician places his weight on the other end, and gently pulls until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper place.
And if you wish, open the ring which is revolved on the hand; and place yourself on the ground with the patient's head at the end of two poles, the length of each being one arm's length, and behind his head place two similar poles in the same manner. The distance between each pole should be a palm's length, and ensure that each ring is firmly attached to the ground so that it does not move.
And there should be one round pole in which is the ring, and it should be revolved in the two rings at the ends of the two poles.
And at the end of it is a ring in which the pole is formed, the length of which is one palm where it revolves; and on the two other poles there is something similar to that.
Unbind the bandages that you have tightly applied to the patient’s feet at the time when you were supposed to remove his head covering, and the bandages that you have tightly applied to his armpits at the time when you were supposed to remove his foot coverings.
At each turn, there should be a minister who holds the turn with his hand, and the surgeon should straighten the spine as we have said.
And once the spine is returned to its proper position and the area has been straightened, now it is necessary to apply an inflammatory plaster on a cloth of henbane, and then stoppered; and afterwards place over the plaster strips of wood whose width should be three fingers or around that size, and the length should cover the area affected by inflammation, and above the part of the spine.
And then bind it with the bandage we have mentioned, and let the patient use delicate nourishment until he is healed.
And if there is any inflammation in the area at the end of the healing process, now it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and soothe over a long period, as we have recommended; and a piece of lead may also be applied to that location.
And sometimes inflammation occurs at the end of the vertebrae in the back, and it is caused by that dislocation; and that bone is one for which caution must be exercised when applying this medicine, because it could easily lead to fatal consequences.
Chapter XXXI.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the attachment of the muscle are involved only in dislocation and not in what affects other joints with slight movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways, which are dislocation towards the interior parts, or towards the exterior parts, or towards the front parts, or towards the rear parts.
And it is most commonly dislocated towards the interior parts, and rarely towards the front parts or towards the rear parts.
And the sign of dislocation towards the interior parts is that when you adjust the leg of the patient on the affected side compared to the healthy side, it is longer and the heel is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their leg behind the neck, and this location is where an abscess would be cauterized, so the head of the bone has already reached here.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs towards the exterior parts is that the symptoms of it are contrary to these symptoms.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs towards the front parts is that when raising the leg according to its completion, it does not bend due to pain in the heel, and if they attempt to walk, they cannot do so towards the front; and their urine is retained and their swallowing is difficult, and they must walk on their heels.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation occurs towards the rear parts is that they cannot raise the heel, nor can they bend the part in front that bends the throat, and their leg is shorter than the other, and swallowing is difficult, and the head of the bone is at that location.
The final reduction of these types of dislocation is that you consider, at this time, if the dislocation is old and ancient, which already has been there for some time, then you administer reduction according to its position; now it is not in that cure entirely; and thus no hope should be placed in it.
In the case where the dislocation is recent and belongs to one of the four types of dislocations, now you should consider turning the joint and extending it to both sides within and without, and move it from side to side, for it easily returns to its place and does not require other treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, now you should prepare strong assistants and extend the limb of the patient from below or at its base, or use a bandage that lies above the limb near the root; and another assistant extends the hand of the patient upwards so as to place their hands below the joints; and then apply a lion's bandage tightly over the joint, and have another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And this extension of the limb or the part inside towards the angle of the shoulder, or from behind to the side.
And this extension of all these should be done at once while the patient is raised off the ground and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is common to all four types.
And thus if the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, now it is necessary for all types what I will say about its proper treatment.
In the reduction of the shoulder when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is towards the inner parts, the patient should be placed on their back; then place the bandages over the armpit in the spot between the head of the humerus and the position below the clavicle; then extend the bandage from under the axilla to as far out as possible along the collarbone.
And then another strong minister should take hold of his arms, and firmly grasp the large part of the dislocated shoulder and pull outward forcefully, for that causes it to return to its place.
And this particular type is the easiest to cure among those in which this member is reduced compared to the other types.
But if it is excused upon you and you do not obey the exercise regimen for this type of treatment at all, then now it is necessary to bind both sides of the patient with a strong, light bandage on both heels and both toes.
And let the length of each be four fingers, and let the diseased shoulder be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended above the top of the post and held by the hand; and let the distance from him to the ground be two arm lengths.
And then command a strong young man to pull on the head of the humerus, and let another young man hold the patient, while the other young man pulls the arm with great strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the reduction of the shoulder when it is dislocated towards the outer part, the patient should lie on his back as we have recommended for treating injuries; and the bandage should be tightly wrapped around the affected shoulder properly and over the feet.
Place the poles as follows: one at the feet and the other at the head; then place adjusted poles on each side of the ridge that has been set up, covered with moist cloths so that the patient is not harmed by the pole being among the objects beneath him, for you must prevent the extension from being pulled down in the lower parts.
And each attendant should extend to his side, and the surgeon, with his hand, will adjust the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then the board should be extended quickly, and it follows this described narration about the ridge; and the surgeon places the palm of his right hand on the patient's side, and with that compression uses the other hand to extend it to the rear part towards the back.
And if the dislocation is in the rear parts, then it does not need for the patient to be extended to the rear parts and raised off the ground; rather, they should be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary when the hip joint is dislocated outwardly, as we have said about positioning the body between two boards; and it is on their back with the limbs extended, as we have said, for there is no harm.
And it is necessary to administer compression with the board at the location of the joint.
Thus, when the reduction is complete as it should be — and the sign of the reduction of the bone is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's limb and when you see those equalities and the patient extends his or her good limb and expands this for an excuse, then you know that the member as it should be has been returned — and then you adjust two things and pause with bandaging and extend a bandage so that the width is not diminished in any of the parts; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or four.
And then loosen the bandage and the dressing, and compare one limb with the other.
Now, if you see those equalities honestly, then you know that the dislocation has already been fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any sign of inflammation, then you return and dress that area, and extend it according to your initial structure, and let it be for three days.
And then loosen that area, and have the patient delay in walking on it for several days until it is comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder.
The shoulder can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated outward, inward, and downward toward the back; it cannot be dislocated forward.
And the sign of shoulder dislocation is that you command the patient to apply their limb to the body; then you know that the shoulder is dislocated.
And for the restoration of all kinds of this is that you should cut open the patient correctly; thus spread out his thigh or leg if possible. Stick your hand to his face and place your foot between his thighs, and then attach the end of his penis with your palms, and hold those firmly over the end of his penis with strength, and another assistant stretches his leg so that the penis returns to its place.
And the sign of recovery for him is that his leg adheres to the body without laborious effort.
And then apply a plaster to that and make the leg adhere to the body, and then bind those together with a bandage for three days or four, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days until he is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of it by you is unnecessary, now administer strong extension with the instruments of which in the treatment of the hip preceding narration.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the thigh.
The thigh sometimes is displaced by slight movement, and sometimes is dislocated completely, and the dislocation of it is done either towards the interior parts or towards the exterior parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the thigh swollen towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of the slight displacement is easy restoration, and it should be extended with ease by pressure and should be bandaged so that it returns to its place.
In the treatment of the hand when it is dislocated for the second time, make sure to have the patient lie straight, and let there be a strong and capable attendant behind him at his mid-side.
And place yourself with your right hand on the uppermost part of his foot, and with your left hand on the lower part of his foot at the location of the joint.
And pull the foot towards yourself with your right hand, and grasp it with your left hand for two moments, and then extend your left hand; and press the bones of the foot with your right hand gently but firmly.
And do this two times as we have described.
And then press the bones of the foot gently but firmly for the third time, and you should be at the joint.
If the hand returns to its proper position in one attempt or two following this method, and if the movement of the hand is equalized, it is good; otherwise, immediately repeat the operation, as it has not returned.
But if you are prevented from reducing it as we have recommended, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and set a strong roller firmly in place between his shoulders, and let him rest against it, and you should wrap a cloth around him so that he does not move.
And the attendant holding the patient’s body, and another attendant extends the foot with his hands or with a strap that lies across the heel of the foot; and then each attendant pulls against the extension on their side, and the roller should be positioned between the patient's sides restraining him, so that his body is not drawn towards the parts behind during the extension.
Here is the translation:
And so when the dislocation is returned and the health of its reduction is declared to you, pause the plaster and wrap it very tightly, and tie the limb to the bench.
And be careful to avoid or protect the nerves which are behind on the heel, so that the bandage over them is not too tight and does not injure them severely.
And then leave that for two days or three; and if the bandage has softened, wrap it again.
And then untie that on the third day or on the fourth, and forbid the patient from moving for forty days. But if the patient is forced to move before that time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not dissolve and become corrupted, and after that it will not receive proper treatment.
And if an abscess occurs that requires cauterization, then you should administer according to the guidelines of our narration in another place concerning treatment and application, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Said Ankle Bones.
Ensure that the one who is dislocated lies with little extension without working; and this is not difficult, but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in some bones of either foot, then you should have the patient lie on the ground or on a table in a position as if they were sitting.
And then you stand on one foot, and place your weight over the area where the joints are, and then press with your foot firmly until it returns to its proper position, and ensure that it appears level and does not protrude in an elevated location.
After the pause, place the foot on a board that will span the entire foot, which has two ends, and then strengthen that with proper structure and firmness for three days; then untie that and allow it to rest with movement for ten days, until it is strengthened and its recovery is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
About the kinds of dislocation that are made by injury or fracture or any combination thereof.
When some cause of those occurs, and you persevere in the treatment of it and in its restoration, many times death results.
And because of this, one should not trust in the treatment of it unless it is a wise person in the art, with long practice, merciful and pious, neither hasty nor presumptuous; and who uses at the beginning medicines that only reduce swellings, and leaves the patient to divine judgment, but to him whom you hope will bring salvation from perdition, or relief from illness, and in whom appears some hope. And then strive to perform the reduction for him at the beginning of the cause, before it becomes a swollen abscess.
And then if the limb returns as we want, immediately administer a regimen that reduces swelling and treats the injury with what is appropriate from among dry ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and occurs in bones that are separated, then you should manage their stabilization, and do as we have said regarding simple injuries, dealing with them in the places where they occurred.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the instrument in the ways of fear as I have preceded my preaching; for this is better for you to serve your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more healthful will be your true body which you shall see in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which God be blessed. Amen.
